$$\{(\llbracket \mathbf{A} \rrbracket_i, \llbracket \mathbf{S}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{A} \rrbracket_i)\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}} \approx \{(\llbracket \mathbf{A} \rrbracket_i, \llbracket \mathbf{C}^{\mathsf{T}} \rrbracket_i)\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}} \text{ for } \mathbf{A} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathbb{Z}_{p(\lambda)}^{k \times \ell(\lambda)}, \mathbf{S} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathbb{Z}_{p(\lambda)}^{k \times q(\lambda)}, \mathbf{C} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathbb{Z}_{p(\lambda)}^{\ell(\lambda) \times q(\lambda)}.$$

When  $\ell$  is omitted, it is implicit that  $\ell(\lambda) = k+1$ , and when q is omitted, it is implicit that  $q(\lambda) = 1$ . It is known that the k-Lin assumption implies  $\text{MDDH}_k$ , which will be the main assumption we base our schemes on. Moreover,  $\text{MDDH}_k$  implies  $\text{MDDH}_{k,\ell}^q$  for any  $\ell, q$ :

**Lemma 2** ([EHK<sup>+</sup>13]). For any polynomial  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$  and  $q = q(\lambda)$ , the MDDH<sup>q</sup><sub>k, $\ell$ </sub> assumption in  $G_i$  (i = 1, 2, T) is reducible to MDDH<sub>k</sub> with a multiplicative security loss min $\{\ell, q\}$  plus an additive security loss O(1/p).

We choose to present more complex schemes using the SXDH assumption (DDH assumption in both  $G_1, G_2$ ).

<span id="page-19-3"></span>**Definition 3** (DDH). For a sequence of pairing groups  $\mathcal{G}$  of order  $p(\lambda)$ , DDH holds in  $G_i$  (i = 1, 2, T) if  $\{[a, b, ab]_i\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}} \approx \{[a, b, c]_i\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  for  $a, b, c \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p(\lambda)}$ .

<u>Remarks.</u> The above definition is DDH with respect to the fixed generator and MDDH<sub>1</sub> is DDH with respect to a random generator. The latter is implied by the former [EHK<sup>+</sup>13], and the recent work of [BMZ19] separates the two in the generic group model. Our schemes do not rely on the generator being fixed (they generalize to  $MDDH_k$ ), yet fixing the generator helps focusing on the high-level ideas.

## <span id="page-19-0"></span>3.3 Attribute-Based Encryption

<span id="page-19-1"></span>**Definition 4.** Let  $\mathcal{M} = \{M_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$  be a sequence of message sets. Let  $\mathcal{P} = \{\mathcal{P}_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$  be a sequence of families of predicates, where  $\mathcal{P}_{\lambda} = \{P : X_P \times Y_P \to \{0,1\}\}$ . An attribute-based encryption (ABE) scheme for message space  $\mathcal{M}$  and predicate space  $\mathcal{P}$  consists of 4 efficient algorithms:

- Setup $(1^{\lambda}, P \in \mathcal{P}_{\lambda})$  generates a pair of master public/secret key (mpk, msk).
- $\bullet \ \operatorname{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}, \operatorname{msk}, y \in Y_P) \ generates \ a \ secret \ key \ \operatorname{sk}_y \ associated \ with \ y.$
- $\operatorname{Enc}(1^{\lambda}, \operatorname{mpk}, x \in X_P, g \in M_{\lambda})$  generates a ciphertext  $\operatorname{ct}_{x,g}$  for g associated with x.
- $Dec(1^{\lambda}, sk, ct)$  outputs either  $\perp$  or a message in  $M_{\lambda}$ .

Correctness requires that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all  $P \in \mathcal{P}_{\lambda}, g \in M_{\lambda}$ , and all  $y \in Y_P, x \in X_P$  such that P(x,y) = 1,

$$\Pr \begin{bmatrix} (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, P) \\ \mathsf{sk} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, y) \, : \, \mathsf{Dec}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}) = g \\ \mathsf{ct} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Enc}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{mpk}, x, g) \end{bmatrix} = 1.$$

The basic security requirement of an ABE scheme is IND-CPA security, which stipulates that no information about the message can be inferred as long as each individual secret key the adversary receives does not allow decryption.

<span id="page-19-2"></span>**Definition 5** (IND-CPA for ABE). Let (Setup, KeyGen, Enc, Dec) be an ABE for message space  $\mathcal{M}$  and predicate space  $\mathcal{P}$ . The scheme is IND-CPA secure if  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{\mathsf{CPA}} \approx \mathsf{Exp}^1_{\mathsf{CPA}}$ , where  $\mathsf{Exp}^b_{\mathsf{CPA}}$  for  $b \in \{0,1\}$  is defined as follows:

• Setup. Run the adversary  $\mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda})$  and receive a predicate  $P \in \mathcal{P}_{\lambda}$  from it. Run (mpk, msk)  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup( $1^{\lambda}, P$ ) and return mpk to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

- Query I. Repeat the following for arbitrarily many rounds determined by A: In each round, A submits y<sup>q</sup> ∈ Y<sup>P</sup> for a secret key. Upon this query, run sk<sup>q</sup> \$← KeyGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , msk, yq) and return sk<sup>q</sup> to A.
- Challenge. A submits a challenge attribute x ∈ X<sup>P</sup> and two messages g0, g<sup>1</sup> ∈ Mλ. Run ct \$← Enc(1<sup>λ</sup> , mpk, x, gb) and return ct to A.
- Query II. Same as Query I.
- Guess. A outputs a bit b 0 . Suppose A makes Q key queries in total. The outcome of the experiment is b 0 if P(x, yq) = 0 for all q ∈ [Q]. Otherwise, the outcome is 0.

## <span id="page-20-0"></span>3.4 Function-Hiding Slotted Inner-Product Functional Encryption

In this paper, we rely on a hybrid variant between secret-key and public-key inner-product functional encryption (IPFE) schemes, called slotted IPFE [\[LV16](#page-83-9)[,Lin17\]](#page-83-7). Here, the index set s of vectors is partitioned into two subsets, spub and spriv, referred to as the public/private slot, respectively. Like in a secret-key IPFE, using the master secret key, one can generate ciphertexts and keys encoding any vectors. In addition, similar to a public-key IPFE, using the master public key, one can generate ciphertexts, however, only for vectors u with values set to zero in the private slot (u|spriv = 0). In other words, master public key allows for encrypting into the public slot only.

We also incorporate the property that the scheme encodes vectors and inner products in the exponent of pairing groups into the definition. As we shall see later, pairing-based slotted IPFE allows us to employ techniques akin to dual system encryption [\[Wat09,](#page-84-3)[LW10](#page-83-11)[,Att16\]](#page-80-0).

<span id="page-20-1"></span>Definition 6 (pairing-based slotted IPFE). Let G be a sequence of pairing groups of order p(λ). A slotted inner-product functional encryption (IPFE) scheme based on G consists of 5 efficient algorithms:

- Setup(1<sup>λ</sup> , spub, spriv) takes as input two disjoint index sets, the public slot spub and the private slot spriv, and outputs a pair of master public key and master secret key (mpk, msk). The whole index set s is spub ∪ spriv.
- KeyGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , msk, [[v]]2) generates a secret key sk<sup>v</sup> for v ∈ Z s p(λ) .
- Enc(1<sup>λ</sup> , msk, [[u]]1) generates a ciphertext ct<sup>u</sup> for u ∈ Z s p(λ) using the master secret key.
- Dec(1<sup>λ</sup> ,skv, ctu) is supposed to compute [[hu, vi]]T.
- SlotEnc(1<sup>λ</sup> , mpk, [[u]]1) generates a ciphertext ct for u| s when given input u ∈ Z spub p(λ) using the master public key.

Decryption correctness requires that for all λ ∈ N, all index set s, and all vectors u, v ∈ Z s p(λ) ,

$$\Pr \begin{bmatrix} \mathsf{msk} & \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, \mathfrak{S}) \\ \mathsf{sk} & \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, \llbracket \mathbf{v} \rrbracket_2) \; : \; \mathsf{Dec}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct}) = \llbracket \langle \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v} \rangle \rrbracket_T \\ \mathsf{ct} & \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{Enc}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, \llbracket \mathbf{u} \rrbracket_1) \end{bmatrix} = 1.$$

Slot-mode correctness requires that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all disjoint index sets  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}}, \mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}}$ , and all vector  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{Z}_{p(\lambda)}^{\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}}}$ , the following distributions should be identical:

$$\begin{cases} (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, \mathfrak{S}_{\mathrm{pub}}, \mathfrak{S}_{\mathrm{priv}}) \\ \mathsf{ct} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Enc}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, \llbracket \mathbf{u} \rrbracket^{\mathfrak{s}} \rrbracket_{1}) \\ \\ (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, \mathfrak{S}_{\mathrm{pub}}, \mathfrak{S}_{\mathrm{priv}}) \\ \mathsf{ct} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{SlotEnc}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{mpk}, \llbracket \mathbf{u} \rrbracket_{1}) \\ \end{cases} : (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}, \mathsf{ct}) \end{cases} .$$

Slotted IPFE generalizes both secret-key and public-key IPFEs: A secret-key IPFE can be obtained by setting  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}} = \emptyset$  and  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}} = \mathfrak{s}$ ; a public-key IPFE can be obtained by setting  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}} = \mathfrak{s}$  and  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}} = \emptyset$ .

We now define the adaptive function-hiding property.

<span id="page-21-2"></span>**Definition 7** (function-hiding slotted IPFE). Let (Setup, KeyGen, Enc, Dec, SlotEnc) be a slotted IPFE. The scheme is function-hiding if  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{\mathrm{FH}} \approx \mathsf{Exp}^1_{\mathrm{FH}}$ , where  $\mathsf{Exp}^b_{\mathrm{FH}}$  for  $b \in \{0,1\}$  is defined as follows:

- Setup. Run the adversary  $\mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda})$  and receive two disjoint index sets  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}}, \mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}}$  from  $\mathcal{A}$ . Let  $\mathfrak{s} = \mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}} \cup \mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}}$ . Run (mpk, msk)  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup( $1^{\lambda}, \mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}}, \mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}}$ ) and return mpk to  $\mathcal{A}$ .
- Challenge. Repeat the following for arbitrarily many rounds determined by A: In each round, A has 2 options.
  - $\mathcal{A}$  can submit  $[\![\mathbf{v}_j^0]\!]_2, [\![\mathbf{v}_j^1]\!]_2$  for a secret key, where  $\mathbf{v}_j^0, \mathbf{v}_j^1 \in \mathbb{Z}_p^5$ . Upon this query, run  $\mathsf{sk}_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^\lambda, \mathsf{msk}, [\![\mathbf{v}_j^b]\!]_2)$  and return  $\mathsf{sk}_j$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .
  - $\mathcal{A}$  can submit  $[\![\mathbf{u}_i^0]\!]_1, [\![\mathbf{u}_i^1]\!]_1$  for a ciphertext, where  $\mathbf{u}_i^0, \mathbf{u}_i^1 \in \mathbb{Z}_p^5$ . Upon this query, run  $\mathsf{ct}_i \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Enc}(1^\lambda, \mathsf{msk}, [\![\mathbf{u}_i^b]\!]_1)$  and return  $\mathsf{ct}_i$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .
- Guess.  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs a bit b'. The outcome is b' if  $\mathbf{v}_j^0|_{\mathfrak{g}_{\text{pub}}} = \mathbf{v}_j^1|_{\mathfrak{g}_{\text{pub}}}$  for all j and  $\langle \mathbf{u}_i^0, \mathbf{v}_j^0 \rangle = \langle \mathbf{u}_i^1, \mathbf{v}_j^1 \rangle$  for all i, j. Otherwise, the outcome is 0.

<span id="page-21-1"></span>**Lemma 8** ([ALS16,Wee17,LV16,Lin17]). Let  $\mathcal{G}$  be a sequence of pairing groups and  $k \geq 1$  an integer constant. If MDDH<sub>k</sub> holds in both  $G_1, G_2$ , then there is an (adaptively) function-hiding slotted IPFE scheme based on  $\mathcal{G}$ .

We present the construction in Appendix A, which is inspired by the selectively secure slotted IPFE scheme from SXDH in [LV16,Lin17].

# <span id="page-21-0"></span>4 Computation Models

We will be considering computation models computing arithmetic functions, i.e., functions with domain  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}}$  for some prime p and index set  $\mathcal{I}$  and with codomain  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ . Since we will construct ABE schemes for certain arithmetic functions, we first associate them with predicates. There are two natural ways of associating arithmetic functions with predicates — whether or not the output is zero, and whether or not the output is non-zero.

**Definition 9** (zero-test predicates). Let  $f: \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \mathbb{Z}_p$  be a function, define the zero-test predicates for f as

$$f_{\neq 0}: \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \{0, 1\}, \mathbf{x} \mapsto \begin{cases} 0, & f(\mathbf{x}) = 0; \\ 1, & f(\mathbf{x}) \neq 0; \end{cases} \qquad f_{=0}: \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \{0, 1\}, \mathbf{x} \mapsto \neg f_{\neq 0}(\mathbf{x}).$$

### <span id="page-22-0"></span>4.1 Arithmetic Branching Programs

Arithmetic branching program[12](#page-22-1) (ABP) is a computation model introduced by Nisan [\[Nis91\]](#page-84-10) and later studied in [\[BG98,](#page-80-9)[IK97,](#page-82-9)[IK00](#page-82-10)[,IK02,](#page-82-6)[IW14\]](#page-82-1). We focus on the variant over Z<sup>p</sup> in this paper.

Definition 10. An arithmetic branching program (ABP) over Z I p is a weighted directed acyclic graph (V, E, w, s, t) with two distinguished vertices, where V is the set of vertices, E is the set of edges, w : E → (Z I <sup>p</sup> → Zp) specifies an affine weight function for each edge, and s, t ∈ V are the distinguished vertices. The in-degree of s and the out-degree of t are 0. Such a program computes a function f : Z I <sup>p</sup> → Z<sup>p</sup> defined by

$$f(\mathbf{x}) = \sum_{\substack{s-t \text{ path} \\ e_1 \cdots e_i}} \prod_{j=1}^i w(e_j)(\mathbf{x}).$$

The size of the ABP is |V |, the number of vertices. Lastly, denote by ABP the class of all ABPs:

$$\mathsf{ABP} = \left\{ f \, \big| \, f \text{ is an ABP over } \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \text{ for some prime } p \text{ and index set } \mathcal{I} \right\}.$$

When there is no ambiguity, we use f to represent both the ABP and the function it computes, and ABP both the class of all ABPs and the class of all functions computed by some ABP.

ABP as Restricted Multiplication. Alternatively, one can think of an ABP as a straight-line program with addition and restricted multiplication where one multiplicand must be affine in the input. Let f = (V, E, w, s, t) be some ABP of size m. Let s = v0, v1, . . . , vm−<sup>1</sup> = t be the vertices sorted topologically. The function f(x) can be computed by the following recurrence:

$$y_0 = 1,$$
  $y_j = \sum_{(v_i, v_j) \in E} w(v_i, v_j)(\mathbf{x}) \cdot y_i,$   $f(\mathbf{x}) = y_{m-1}.$ 

Note that in the recurrence relation, we have i < j by the sorting.

ABP as Determinant. It is known [\[IK97](#page-82-9)[,IK02\]](#page-82-6) that one can compute an ABP by computing the determinant of a special matrix:

<span id="page-22-2"></span>Lemma 11 (Lemma 1 in [\[IK02\]](#page-82-6)). Let f = (V, E, w, s, t) be an ABP of size m and s = v0, v1, . . . , vm−<sup>1</sup> = t be sorted topologically. Let M be an (m − 1) × (m − 1) matrix whose entries are

$$\mathbf{M}[i+1,j] = \begin{cases} 0, & i > j; \\ -1, & i = j; \\ 0, & i < j, (v_i, v_j) \notin E; \\ w(v_i, v_j)(\mathbf{x}), & i < j, (v_i, v_j) \in E. \end{cases}$$

Then the entries of M are affine in x and f(x) = detM.

<span id="page-22-1"></span><sup>12</sup>The model is also known as algebraic branching programs or mod-p counting branching programs, though the precise definitions vary.

### <span id="page-23-0"></span>4.2 Turing Machines

The other computation model we consider in this work is Turing machines with a read-only input tape and a read-write work tape. This type of Turing machine is used to define space-bounded complexity classes of decision problems, and in this work, we construct an ABE scheme for uniform logspace predicates. One tricky issue with handling Turing machines is that the time and space complexity of a Turing machine is uncomputable. However, our ABE construction can only handle computation with polynomial time complexity and logarithmic space complexity. Below we define time/space bounded computation of a Turing machine. Correspondingly, in our ABE scheme, the attribute specifies, in addition to an input to the Turing machine, upper bounds on the concrete time complexity T and space complexity S, and decryption succeeds if and only if the machine accepts the input within this time/space bound. We will also confine ourselves to the binary alphabet. The definition below captures these features explicitly.

<span id="page-23-1"></span>**Definition 12** (Turing machine & time/space bounded computation). A (deterministic) Turing machine (over  $\{0,1\}$ ) is a triplet  $M=(Q,\mathbf{y}_{\mathrm{acc}},\delta)$ , where  $Q\geq 1$  is the number of states (we use [Q] as the set of states and 1 as the initial state),  $\mathbf{y}_{\mathrm{acc}}\in\{0,1\}^Q$  indicates whether each state is accepting, and

$$\delta: [Q] \times \{0,1\} \times \{0,1\} \to [Q] \times \{0,1\} \times \{0,\pm 1\} \times \{0,\pm 1\},$$
$$(q,x,w) \mapsto (q',w',\Delta i,\Delta j)$$

is the state transition function, which, given the current state q, the symbol x on the input tape under scan, and the symbol w on the work tape under scan, specifies the new state q', the symbol w' overwriting w, the direction  $\Delta i$  to which the input tape pointer moves, and the direction  $\Delta j$  to which the work tape pointer moves. The machine is required to hang (instead of halting) once it reaches an accepting state, i.e., for all  $q \in [Q]$  such that  $\mathbf{y}_{acc}[q] = 1$  and all  $x, w \in \{0, 1\}$ , it holds that  $\delta(q, x, w) = (q, w, 0, 0)$ .

For input length  $N \geq 1$  and space complexity bound  $S \geq 1$ , the set of internal configurations of M is

$$C_{M,N,S} = [N] \times [S] \times \{0,1\}^S \times [Q],$$

where  $(i, j, \mathbf{W}, q) \in \mathcal{C}_{M,N,S}$  specifies the input tape pointer  $i \in [N]$ , the work tape pointer  $j \in [S]$ , the content of the work tape  $\mathbf{W} \in \{0,1\}^S$  and the machine state  $q \in [Q]$ . For any bit-string  $\mathbf{x} \in \{0,1\}^N$  for  $N \geq 1$  and time/space complexity bounds  $T, S \geq 1$ , the

For any bit-string  $\mathbf{x} \in \{0,1\}^N$  for  $N \geq 1$  and time/space complexity bounds  $T, S \geq 1$ , the machine M accepts  $\mathbf{x}$  within time T and space S if there exists a sequence of internal configurations (computation path of T steps)  $c_0, \ldots, c_T \in \mathcal{C}_{M,N,S}$  with  $c_t = (i_t, j_t, \mathbf{W}_t, q_t)$  such that

$$i_0 = 1, j_0 = 1, \mathbf{W}_0 = \mathbf{0}_S, q_0 = 1 \quad \text{(initial configuration)};$$
 for  $0 \le t < T$ : 
$$\begin{cases} \delta(q_t, \mathbf{x}[i_t], \mathbf{W}_t[j_t]) = (q_{t+1}, \mathbf{W}_{t+1}[j_t], i_{t+1} - i_t, j_{t+1} - j_t), \\ \mathbf{W}_{t+1}[j] = \mathbf{W}_t[j] \text{ for all } j \ne j_t \quad \text{(valid transitions)}; \\ \mathbf{y}_{\text{acc}}[q_T] = 1 \quad \text{(accepting)}. \end{cases}$$

Denote by  $M|_{N,T,S}$  the function  $\{0,1\}^N \to \{0,1\}$  mapping  $\mathbf{x}$  to whether M accepts  $\mathbf{x}$  in time T and space S. Define  $\mathsf{TM} = \{M \mid M \text{ is a Turing machine}\}$  to be the set of all Turing machines.

<u>Remarks.</u> The above definition disallows moving off the input/work tape. For example, if  $\delta$  specifies moving the input tape pointer to the left when it is already at the leftmost position, there is no valid next internal configuration.

We can avoid the problem of moving off the input tape by encoding the input string  $ab\cdots cd$  as  $1a0b0\cdots 0c0d1$ . Here, the 1's at the ends indicate moving to one direction (the last direction to which the input tape pointer moved, or left initially) should be avoided, and the interleaving 0's indicate that both directions are allowed. The machine uses 2 additional bits in its state to remember

- whether it is reading an indicator bit or an input bit (initially "indicator"), and
- to which direction the input tape pointer last moved (initially "left").

The machine can maintain those bits and avoid moving off the input tape accordingly.

We can also eliminate the problem of moving off the work tape to the left by encoding  $ab \cdots cd$  on the work tape as  $1a0b0\cdots0c0d000\cdots$ , where the leading 1 indicates moving to the left should be avoided, and the interleaving 0's indicate both directions are allowed. The machine uses one additional bit in its state and avoids moving off the work tape to the left accordingly.

The problem of moving off the work tape to the right is undetectable by the machine, and this is intended. When the space bound is violated, the input is silently rejected.

Relations Among Computation Models. A Boolean (resp. arithmetic) formula can be converted to a Boolean (resp. arithmetic) branching program in linear time, and a Boolean branching program (BP) can be trivially converted into an ABP. Therefore, ABP is a model that generalizes all the three without degrade in efficiency. Moreover, if a non-deterministic BP is unambiguous (i.e., there is at most one accepting path), it can be trivially converted to an ABP; otherwise, it can be approximated by an ABP of the same size with random weights. Lastly, a family of polynomial-sized ABPs can decide languages in NL/poly [BG98]. Note that this does not mean ABE for ABP trivially implies ABE for NL. In the former, each secret key is associated with a specific ABP instance thus only works for a specific input length, whereas in the latter, each secret key is associated with a Turing machine and can work for any input length.

# <span id="page-24-0"></span>5 Arithmetic Key Garbling Scheme

Arithmetic key garbling scheme (AKGS) is an information-theoretic primitive related to randomized encodings [AIK11] and partial garbling schemes [IW14]. It is the information-theoretic core in our construction of one-key one-ciphertext ABE (more precisely 1-ABE constructed in Section 6.2). Given a function  $f: \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \mathbb{Z}_p$  and two secrets  $\alpha, \beta \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , an AKGS produces label functions  $L_1, \ldots, L_m: \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \mathbb{Z}_p$  that are affine in  $\mathbf{x}$ . For any  $\mathbf{x}$ , one can compute  $\alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta$  from  $L_1(\mathbf{x}), \ldots, L_m(\mathbf{x})$  together with f and  $\mathbf{x}$ , while all other information about  $\alpha, \beta$  are hidden.

<span id="page-24-1"></span>**Definition 13** (AKGS, adopted from Definition 1 in [IW14]). An arithmetic key garbling scheme (AKGS) for a function class  $\mathcal{F} = \{f\}$ , where  $f : \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \mathbb{Z}_p$  for some  $p, \mathcal{I}$  specified by f, consists of two efficient algorithms:

- Garble( $f \in \mathcal{F}, \alpha \in \mathbb{Z}_p, \beta \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ ) is randomized and outputs m affine functions  $L_1, \ldots, L_m$ :  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \mathbb{Z}_p$  (called label functions, which specifies how input is encoded as labels). Pragmatically, it outputs the coefficient vectors  $\mathbf{L}_1, \ldots, \mathbf{L}_m$ .
- Eval $(f \in \mathcal{F}, \mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}}, \ell_1 \in \mathbb{Z}_p, \dots, \ell_m \in \mathbb{Z}_p)$  is deterministic and outputs a value in  $\mathbb{Z}_p$  (the input  $\ell_1, \dots, \ell_m$  are called labels, which are supposed to be the values of the label functions at  $\mathbf{x}$ ).

Correctness requires that for all f : Z I <sup>p</sup> → Z<sup>p</sup> ∈ F, α, β ∈ Zp, x ∈ Z I p ,

$$\Pr\left[ \frac{(\mathbf{L}_1, \dots, \mathbf{L}_m) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Garble}(f, \alpha, \beta)}{\ell_j \leftarrow L_j(\mathbf{x}) \text{ for } j \in [m]} : \mathsf{Eval}(f, \mathbf{x}, \ell_1, \dots, \ell_m) = \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta \right] = 1.$$

We also require that the scheme have deterministic shape, meaning that m is determined solely by f, independent of α, β and the randomness in Garble. The number of label functions, m, is called the garbling size of f under this scheme.

<span id="page-25-1"></span>Definition 14 (linear AKGS). An AKGS (Garble, Eval) for F is linear if the following conditions hold:

- Garble(f, α, β) uses a uniformly random vector r \$← Z m<sup>0</sup> <sup>p</sup> as its randomness, where m<sup>0</sup> is determined solely by f, independent of α, β.
- The coefficient vectors L1, . . . ,L<sup>m</sup> produced by Garble(f, α, β; r) are linear in (α, β, r).
- Eval(f, x, `1, . . . , `m) is linear in (`1, . . . , `m).

Later in this paper, AKGS refers to linear AKGS by default.

### <span id="page-25-0"></span>5.1 Security Notions of AKGS

The basic security notion of AKGS requires the existence of an efficient simulator that draws a sample from the real labels' distribution given f, x, αf(x) + β. We emphasize, as it's the same case in [\[IW14\]](#page-82-1), that AKGS does not hide x and hides all other information about α, β except the value αf(x) + β.

Definition 15 ((usual) simulation security, Definition 1 in [\[IW14\]](#page-82-1)). An AKGS (Garble, Eval) for F is secure if there exists an efficient algorithm Sim such that for all f : Z I <sup>p</sup> → Z<sup>p</sup> ∈ F, α, β ∈ Zp, x ∈ Z I p , the following distributions are identical:

$$\begin{cases} (\mathbf{L}_1, \dots, \mathbf{L}_m) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Garble}(f, \alpha, \beta) \\ \ell_j \leftarrow L_j(\mathbf{x}) \text{ for } j \in [m] \end{cases} : (\ell_1, \dots, \ell_m) \end{cases},$$
 
$$\{ (\ell_1, \dots, \ell_m) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Sim}(f, \mathbf{x}, \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta) : (\ell_1, \dots, \ell_m) \}.$$

As discussed in Section [2.1,](#page-6-0) the usual simulation security suffices for selective (or semi-adaptive) security. To achieve adaptive security, we need the following stronger property.

<span id="page-25-2"></span>Definition 16 (piecewise security). An AKGS (Garble, Eval) for F is piecewise secure if the following conditions hold:

• The first label is reversely sampleable from the other labels together with f and x. This reconstruction is perfect even given all the other label functions. Formally, there exists an efficient algorithm RevSamp such that for all f : Z I <sup>p</sup> → Z<sup>p</sup> ∈ F, α, β ∈ Zp, x ∈ Z I p , the following distributions are identical:

$$\begin{cases} (\mathbf{L}_{1},\ldots,\mathbf{L}_{m}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Garble}(f,\alpha,\beta) \\ & \ell_{1} \leftarrow L_{1}(\mathbf{x}) \end{cases} & : (\ell_{1},\mathbf{L}_{2},\ldots,\mathbf{L}_{m}) \end{cases},$$
 
$$\begin{cases} (\mathbf{L}_{1},\ldots,\mathbf{L}_{m}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Garble}(f,\alpha,\beta) \\ & \ell_{j} \leftarrow L_{j}(\mathbf{x}) \text{ for } j \in [m], j > 1 \\ & \ell_{1} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{RevSamp}(f,\mathbf{x},\alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta,\ell_{2},\ldots,\ell_{m}) \end{cases} : (\ell_{1},\mathbf{L}_{2},\ldots,\mathbf{L}_{m}) \end{cases}.$$

• For the other labels, each is marginally random even given all the label functions after it. Formally, this means for all  $f: \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \mathbb{Z}_p \in \mathcal{F}, \alpha, \beta \in \mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}}$  and all  $j \in [m], j > 1$ , the following distributions are identical:

$$\begin{cases} (\mathbf{L}_{1}, \dots, \mathbf{L}_{m}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Garble}(f, \alpha, \beta) \\ \ell_{j} \leftarrow L_{j}(\mathbf{x}) \end{cases} : (\ell_{j}, \mathbf{L}_{j+1}, \dots, \mathbf{L}_{m}) \end{cases}, \\ \begin{cases} (\mathbf{L}_{1}, \dots, \mathbf{L}_{m}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Garble}(f, \alpha, \beta) \\ \ell_{j} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p} \end{cases} : (\ell_{j}, \mathbf{L}_{j+1}, \dots, \mathbf{L}_{m}) \end{cases}.$$

<span id="page-26-0"></span>**Lemma 17.** A piecewise secure AKGS (Garble, Eval) for some function class  $\mathcal{F}$  is also secure.

<u>Proof.</u> Assuming the AKGS is piecewise secure, we let the simulator  $\mathsf{Sim}(f,\mathbf{x},\gamma)$  sample  $\ell_j \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  for  $1 < j \leq m$ , reversely sample  $\ell_1 \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{RevSamp}(f,\mathbf{x},\gamma,\ell_2,\ldots,\ell_m)$ , and output  $(\ell_1,\ldots,\ell_m)$ . It is readily verified that Sim efficiently and perfectly simulates the labels. Therefore, the AKGS is also secure.

**Special Piecewise Security.** We identify a special structural form of AKGS, referred to as special piecewise security. It is equivalent to piecewise security, and has the advantage of being easier to verify. In particular, all AKGS schemes considered in this work have this form.

**Definition 18.** An AKGS (Garble, Eval) for  $\mathcal{F}$  is special piecewise secure if it has the following special form:

- For all  $f: \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \mathbb{Z}_p$  in  $\mathcal{F}$  and all  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}}$ ,  $\mathsf{Eval}(f, \mathbf{x}, \overset{\ell_1}{1}, \overset{\ell_2}{0}, \ldots, \overset{\ell_m}{0}) \neq 0$ .
- For all  $f: \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}} \to \mathbb{Z}_p \in \mathcal{F}, \alpha, \beta \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , let  $\mathbf{r} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{m'}$  be the randomness  $\mathsf{Garble}(f, \alpha, \beta)$  uses. For all  $j \in [m], j > 1$ , the label function  $L_j$  produced by  $\mathsf{Garble}(f, \alpha, \beta; \mathbf{r})$  can be written as

$$L_j(\mathbf{x}) = k_j \mathbf{r}[j-1] + L'_j(\mathbf{x}; \alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r}[\geq j]),$$

where  $k_j \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  is a non-zero constant (not depending on  $\mathbf{x}, \alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r}$ ) and  $L'_j$  is an affine function of  $\mathbf{x}$  whose coefficient vector is linear in  $(\alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r}[j], \mathbf{r}[j+1], \ldots)$ . The component  $\mathbf{r}[j-1]$  is called the randomizer of  $L_j$  and  $\ell_j$ .

We note that the first requirement of special piecewise security, on top of Eval being linear in the labels (always required as a linear AKGS), simply says the coefficient of  $\ell_1$  is always non-zero. An AKGS with the special form is clearly piecewise secure.

**Lemma 19.** A special piecewise secure AKGS (Garble, Eval) for some function class  $\mathcal{F}$  is also piecewise secure. Moreover, the RevSamp algorithm (required in piecewise security) obtained for a special piecewise secure AKGS is linear in  $\gamma, \ell_2, \ldots, \ell_m$  and perfectly recovers  $\ell_1$  even if the randomness of Garble is not uniformly sampled.

<u>Proof.</u> Assuming the AKGS is special piecewise secure, we first show that the first label is reversely sampleable. Since Eval is linear in the labels, we have

$$\mathsf{Eval}(f,\mathbf{x},\ell_1,\ell_2,\dots,\ell_m) = \ell_1 \mathsf{Eval}(f,\mathbf{x},1,0,\dots,0) + \mathsf{Eval}(f,\mathbf{x},0,\ell_2,\dots,\ell_m).$$

Define the reverse sampling algorithm as

$$\mathsf{RevSamp}(f,\mathbf{x},\gamma,\ell_2,\ldots,\ell_m) = \big(\mathsf{Eval}(f,\mathbf{x},1,0,\ldots,0)\big)^{-1} \big(\gamma - \mathsf{Eval}(f,\mathbf{x},0,\ell_2,\ldots,\ell_m)\big),$$

which is clearly efficient and linear in  $\gamma, \ell_2, \dots, \ell_m$ . Note that given  $f, \mathbf{x}, \gamma = \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta, \ell_2, \dots, \ell_m$ , the first label  $\ell_1$  is uniquely determined due to the requirement of Eval in special piecewise security, and the algorithm RevSamp defined above computes this value. Since  $\ell_1$  is uniquely determined, it does not matter if the label functions  $\mathbf{L}_2, \dots, \mathbf{L}_m$  are revealed, nor if the garbling is not generated using uniform randomness.

For marginal randomness property, for all  $1 < j \le m$ , given the label functions  $\mathbf{L}_{j+1}, \dots, \mathbf{L}_m$ , the label  $\ell_j = k_j \mathbf{r}[j-1] + L'_j(\mathbf{x})$  is uniformly random since  $\mathbf{r}[j-1]$  is a uniformly random value independent of  $\mathbf{L}'_j, \mathbf{L}_{j+1}, \dots, \mathbf{L}_m$ .

A piecewise secure AKGS can be converted into a special piecewise secure one. We present the proof of the following theorem in Appendix C:

<span id="page-27-3"></span>**Theorem 20.** A piecewise secure AKGS is also special piecewise secure after an appropriate change of variable for the randomness used by Garble.

## <span id="page-27-0"></span>6 ABE for ABPs

In this section, we show how to construct a KP-ABE for ABPs in three steps: *i*) construct a piecewise secure AKGS for ABPs; *ii*) build a 1-key 1-ciphertext secure secret-key ABE (1-ABE) using a piecewise secure AKGS and a function-hiding secret-key IPFE; *iii*) lift the 1-ABE into a full-fledged ABE using slotted IPFE.

The first step is specific to the class of predicates for which we want to construct an ABE. The second and third steps are independent of the predicates as long as all the predicates share the same domain.

### <span id="page-27-1"></span>6.1 AKGS for ABPs

Our piecewise secure AKGS for ABPs is a special case of the partial garbling scheme for ABPs in [IW14], which is in turn built upon randomizing polynomials [IK00,IK02]. In a partial garbling scheme (PGS), the input of a function  $f: \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}_x} \times \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}_z} \to \mathbb{Z}_p$  is divided into two parts, the public part  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}_x}$  and the private part  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}_z}$ . The labels for f with input  $\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{z}$  in a secure PGS contain no other information about  $\mathbf{z}$  except the value  $f(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{z})$ , but might otherwise completely reveal  $\mathbf{x}$ .

More specifically, our AKGS garbling for computation f(x) with secrets  $\alpha, \beta$  is essentially a PGS garbling for the computation  $\alpha f(x) + \beta$ , where x is the public input and  $\alpha, \beta$  are the private inputs. In this special case (slightly more generally, when the computation has degree 1 in the private inputs), the PGS garbling of [IW14] satisfies the linearity structure and the (usual) simulation security of AKGS. We further show that it also satisfies the *piecewise security*.

AKGS and PGS have slightly different syntax. In an AKGS, the label functions  $L_j$ 's are regarded as affine functions of  $\mathbf{x}$  with  $(\alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r})$  hardwired, and the coefficient vectors  $\mathbf{L}_j$  are linear in  $(\alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r})$ . In a PGS,  $L_j$ 's are regarded as affine functions of  $(\mathbf{x}, \alpha, \beta)$  with  $\mathbf{r}$  hardwired, and the coefficient vectors  $\mathbf{L}_j$  are affine in  $\mathbf{r}$ . Below, we recall the PGS for ABPs [IW14] in the syntax of an AKGS.

<span id="page-27-2"></span>Construction 21 (AKGS for ABPs [IW14]). Let  $\mathcal{F} = \mathsf{ABP}$  be the class of all ABPs. The AKGS (Garble, Eval) for  $\mathcal{F}$  operates as follows: (We inline comments to and explanation of the scheme in italics.)

• Garble $(f, \alpha, \beta)$  takes an ABP  $f \in \mathcal{F} = \mathsf{ABP}$  and two secrets  $\alpha, \beta$  as input. Suppose f is over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}}$  and of size m, and  $\alpha, \beta \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ . The algorithm computes the matrix  $\mathbf{M}$  in Lemma 11 (whose

determinant is the output of the function) and augments it into an  $m \times m$  matrix  $\mathbf{M}'$ :

$$\mathbf{M}' = \begin{pmatrix} \begin{bmatrix} * & * & \cdots & * & * & \beta \\ -1 & * & \cdots & * & * & 0 \\ -1 & * & \cdots & * & * & 0 \\ & -1 & \cdots & * & * & 0 \\ & & \ddots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ 0 & & & -1 & * & 0 \\ \hline 0 & 0 & \cdots & 0 & -1 & \alpha \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{M} & \mathbf{m}_1 \\ \mathbf{m}_2^\mathsf{T} & \alpha \end{pmatrix}.$$

Here, the augmented matrix  $\mathbf{M}'$  is the matrix for an ABP computing  $\alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta$ . It then samples  $\mathbf{r} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{m-1}$  as its randomness, sets  $\mathbf{N} = \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{I}_{m-1} & \mathbf{r} \\ \mathbf{0} & 1 \end{pmatrix}$ , and defines the label functions by

$$\widehat{\mathbf{M}} = \mathbf{M}'\mathbf{N} = \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{M} & \mathbf{M}\mathbf{r} + \mathbf{m}_1 \ \mathbf{m}_2^\mathsf{T} & \mathbf{m}_2^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{r} + \alpha \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} & \mathbf{M} & & L_1(\mathbf{x}) \ & \mathbf{M} & & \vdots \ & & & \vdots \ & & & L_{m-1}(\mathbf{x}) \ \end{pmatrix}.$$

The algorithm collects the coefficient vectors  $\mathbf{L}_1, \dots, \mathbf{L}_m$  of  $L_1, \dots, L_m$  and returns them.

Note: We show Garble satisfies the required properties of a linear AKGS (Definitions 13 and 14):

- $-\underline{L}_1, \dots, \underline{L}_m$  are affine functions of  $\mathbf{x}$ :  $\mathbf{M}'$  is affine in  $\mathbf{x}$  and  $\mathbf{N}$  is constant with respect to  $\mathbf{x}$ , therefore, the label functions  $L_1, \dots, L_m$  (the last column of  $\mathbf{M}'\mathbf{N}$ ) are affine in  $\mathbf{x}$ .
- <u>Shape determinism holds:</u> The garbling size of f is its size as an ABP.
- Garble is linear in  $(\alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r})$ , i.e., the coefficients of  $L_1, \ldots, L_m$  are linear in  $(\alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r})$ :  $\mathbf{M}$  is constant with respect to  $(\alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r})$ , and  $\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{m}_1, \alpha$  are linear in  $(\alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r})$ , so  $\mathbf{Mr} + \mathbf{m}_1$  and  $\mathbf{Mr} + \alpha$  (hence the coefficients of  $L_1, \ldots, L_{m-1}$  and  $L_m$ ) are linear in  $(\alpha, \beta, \mathbf{r})$ .
- Eval $(f, \mathbf{x}, \ell_1, \dots, \ell_m)$  takes an ABP  $f \in \mathcal{F} = \mathsf{ABP}$  (over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}}$  and of size m), an input  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathcal{I}}$  and m labels  $\ell_1, \dots, \ell_m$  as input. It computes  $\mathbf{M}$  (with the value of  $\mathbf{x}$  substituted) in Lemma 11 and sets

$$\widehat{\mathbf{M}} = \left( \begin{array}{cccccccccccccccccccccccccccccccccccc$$

The algorithm returns  $\det \widehat{\mathbf{M}}$ .

Note: We show the correctness of the scheme. Since  $\ell_j = L_j(\mathbf{x})$  for all  $j \in [m]$ , the matrix  $\widehat{\mathbf{M}}$  in Eval is the matrix  $\widehat{\mathbf{M}}$  in Garble with  $\mathbf{x}$  plugged into it, and we have

$$\det \widehat{\mathbf{M}} = \det (\mathbf{M}'\mathbf{N}) = \det \mathbf{M}' \det \mathbf{N}$$

$$\begin{pmatrix} \text{Laplace expansion in} \\ \text{the last column of } \mathbf{M}' \end{pmatrix} = \det \mathbf{M}'$$

$$= \alpha \det \mathbf{M} + \beta$$

$$(\text{Lemma 11}) = \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta.$$

Eval is also linear in  $\ell_1, \ldots, \ell_m$ . This follows by the Laplace expansion of  $\det \widehat{\mathbf{M}}$  in the last column.

**Security.** Ishai and Wee [IW14] observed that in Garble in Construction 21, all the possible N's form a matrix subgroup

$$H = \left\{ \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{I}_{m-1} & \mathbf{r} \\ \mathbf{0} & 1 \end{pmatrix} \middle| \mathbf{r} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{m-1} \right\}.$$

Moreover, the labels are the last column of a uniformly random element from the left coset  $\mathbf{M}'H = \{\mathbf{M}'\mathbf{N} \mid \mathbf{N} \in H\}$ . The (usual) simulation security follows from the observation that each such left coset has a canonical representative  $\mathbf{M}''$  determined by  $f, \mathbf{x}$  and  $\alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta$ :

$$\mathbf{M}'' = \begin{pmatrix} & & & & & & & \\ & & & & & & & \\ & & \mathbf{M} & & & & & \\ & & & & & & \vdots & \\ & & & & &$$

By the properties of cosets,  $\mathbf{M}''H = \mathbf{M}'H$ , therefore, given  $f, \mathbf{x}, \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta$ , one can sample a uniformly random element from  $\mathbf{M}''H = \mathbf{M}'H$  to simulate the labels.

We now proceed to proving the stronger security notions introduced in this work.

<span id="page-29-0"></span>**Theorem 22.** Construction 21 is special piecewise secure.

<u>Proof.</u> Adopt the notations in Construction 21. We first show that the coefficient of  $\ell_1$  in Eval is non-zero. Recall that

$$\widehat{\mathbf{M}} = \begin{pmatrix} & & & & & & & & & & & & & & & & & &$$

Consider the Laplace expansion of  $\det \widehat{\mathbf{M}}$  in the the last column:

$$\mathsf{Eval}(f,\mathbf{x},\ell_1,\ldots,\ell_m) = \det \widehat{\mathbf{M}} = A_1\ell_1 + A_2\ell_2 + \cdots + A_m\ell_m,$$

where  $A_j$  is the (j, m)-cofactor of  $\widehat{\mathbf{M}}$ . Observe that by definition, each  $A_j$  is solely determined by  $(f, \mathbf{x})$ , so the above expansion expresses Eval as a linear function of the labels, and in particular, the coefficient of  $\ell_1$  is  $A_1 = (-1)^{1+m}(-1)^{m-1} = 1 \neq 0$ .

Now we show that the label functions are in the special form. Recall that the label functions are defined by the last column of

$$\widehat{\mathbf{M}} = \mathbf{M'N} = \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{M}[1,1] \ \mathbf{M}[1,2] \cdots \mathbf{M}[1,m-2] & \mathbf{M}[1,m-1] & \beta \\ -1 & \mathbf{M}[2,2] \cdots \mathbf{M}[2,m-2] & \mathbf{M}[2,m-1] & 0 \\ & -1 & \cdots \mathbf{M}[3,m-2] & \mathbf{M}[3,m-1] & 0 \\ & \ddots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ & & -1 & \mathbf{M}[m-1,m-1] & 0 \\ & & & -1 & \alpha \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} 1 & \mathbf{r}[1] \\ 1 & \mathbf{r}[2] \\ & 1 & \mathbf{r}[3] \\ & \ddots & \vdots \\ & & 1 \ \mathbf{r}[m-1] \\ & & 1 \end{pmatrix}.$$

Computing the label functions  $L_2, \ldots, L_m$ , we get

for 
$$1 < j < m$$
:  $L_j(\mathbf{x}) = -\mathbf{r}[j-1] + \sum_{j'=j}^{m-1} \mathbf{r}[j']\mathbf{M}[j,j'],$ 

$$L_m(\mathbf{x}) = -\mathbf{r}[m-1] + \sum_{j'=j}^{m-1} \mathbf{r}[j']\mathbf{M}[j,j'],$$

$$L_m(\mathbf{x}) = -\mathbf{r}[m-1] + \alpha.$$

$$\downarrow \qquad \qquad \uparrow \qquad \qquad \uparrow \qquad \qquad \downarrow \qquad \downarrow \qquad \downarrow \qquad \downarrow \qquad \downarrow \qquad \downarrow$$

They are in the required special form (the randomizer of  $L_j$  is  $\mathbf{r}[j-1]$ ).

Combining the two, we conclude that Construction 21 is special piecewise secure.

# <span id="page-30-0"></span>6.2 1-Key 1-Ciphertext Secure Secret-Key ABE

At the core of our adaptively secure ABE is a construction for the simple case of 1-key 1-ciphertext secure secret-key ABE — we call it 1-ABE. (For technical reasons, it is more convenient to define it as a key encapsulation mechanism.) It captures our key ideas for achieving adaptive security using AKGS and function-hiding IPFE while keeping the ciphertext compact. Below we start with defining the syntax and security of 1-ABE.

<span id="page-30-1"></span>**Definition 23.** Let  $\mathcal{G}$  be a sequence of pairing groups of order  $p(\lambda)$ . A 1-ABE scheme based on  $\mathcal{G}$  has the same syntax as an ABE scheme in Definition  $\frac{1}{4}$ , except that

- There is no message space  $\mathcal{M}$ .
- Setup outputs a master secret key msk, without a mpk.
- KeyGen $(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, y, \mu)$  outputs a secret key sk for policy y that encapsulates a pad  $\mu \in \mathbb{Z}_{p(\lambda)}$ .
- $\bullet$  Enc $(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, x)$  uses  $\mathsf{msk}$  and outputs a ciphertext ct for attribute x without encrypting a message.
- $\operatorname{Dec}(\operatorname{sk},\operatorname{ct})$   $\operatorname{outputs} \perp \operatorname{or} \operatorname{some} \ \llbracket \mu' \rrbracket_{\operatorname{T}}.$
- Correctness requires that  $\mu = \mu'$  if the decapsulation should be successful, i.e., P(x,y) = 1.

Such a scheme is 1-key 1-ciphertext secure (or simply secure) if  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{1-\mathsf{sk},1-\mathsf{ct}} \approx \mathsf{Exp}^1_{1-\mathsf{sk},1-\mathsf{ct}}$ , where  $\mathsf{Exp}^b_{1-\mathsf{sk},1-\mathsf{ct}}$  is defined similarly to  $\mathsf{Exp}^b_{CPA}$  in Definition 5:

- In Setup, the adversary A chooses a predicate P but does not receive a mpk.
- In Query I/II, when A submits a key query y, sample two random pads µ 0 , µ<sup>1</sup> \$← Zp(λ) , run sk \$← KeyGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , msk, y, µ<sup>0</sup> ), and return (sk, µ<sup>b</sup> ) to A.
- In Challenge, when A submits a challenge attribute x, run ct \$← Enc(1<sup>λ</sup> , msk, x), and return ct to A.
- In Guess, A outputs a bit b 0 . The outcome of the experiment is b 0 if the adversary makes only a single key query for some y and P(x, y) = 0. Otherwise, the outcome is 0.

For any function class F (e.g., arithmetic branching programs), we show how to construct a 1-ABE for the class of zero-test predicates in F (i.e., predicates of form f6=0, f=0 that computes whether f(x) evaluates to zero or non-zero), using a piecewise secure AKGS for F and a function-hiding secret-key IPFE scheme.

<span id="page-31-0"></span>Construction 24 (1-ABE). We describe the construction for any fixed value of the security parameter λ and suppress the appearance of λ below for simplicity of notations. Let (Garble, Eval) be an AKGS for a function class F, G pairing groups of order p, and (IPFE.Setup, IPFE.KeyGen, IPFE.Enc, IPFE.Dec) a secret-key IPFE based on G. We construct a 1-ABE scheme based on G for the predicate space P induced by F:

$$X_n = \mathbb{Z}_p^n, \quad Y_n = \{ f_{\neq 0}, f_{=0} \mid f \in \mathcal{F}, f : \mathbb{Z}_p^n \to \mathbb{Z}_p \},$$
  
$$\mathcal{P} = \{ P_n : X_n \times Y_n \to \{0, 1\}, (\mathbf{x}, y) \mapsto y(\mathbf{x}) \mid n \in \mathbb{N} \}.$$

The 1-ABE scheme (Setup,KeyGen, Enc, Dec) operates as follows:

• Setup(1<sup>n</sup> ) takes the attribute length in unary (i.e., P<sup>n</sup> is encoded as 1<sup>n</sup> ) as input. It generates an IPFE master secret key msk \$← IPFE.Setup(s1-ABE) for the index set s1-ABE = {const, coef1, . . . , coefn,sim1,sim?}. The algorithm returns msk as the master secret key.

Note: The positions indexed by const, coef1, . . . , coef<sup>n</sup> in the secret key encode the coefficient vectors L<sup>j</sup> of the label functions L<sup>i</sup> produced by garbling f with secrets α, β, and these positions encode (1, x) in the ciphertext. The positions indexed by sim1,sim? are set to zero by the honest algorithms, and are only used in the security proof.

• KeyGen(msk, y ∈ Yn, µ ∈ Zp) samples η \$← Z<sup>p</sup> and garbles the function f underlying y as follows:

$$\begin{cases} \alpha \leftarrow \mu, & \beta \leftarrow 0, & \text{if } y = f_{\neq 0}; \\ \alpha \leftarrow \eta, & \beta \leftarrow \mu, & \text{if } y = f_{=0}; \end{cases}$$
  $(\mathbf{L}_1, \dots, \mathbf{L}_m) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Garble}(f, \alpha, \beta).$ 

It generates an IPFE key isk<sup>j</sup> \$← IPFE.KeyGen(msk, [[v<sup>j</sup> ]]2) for the following vector v<sup>j</sup> encoding each label function L<sup>j</sup> :

| vector | const         | coefi             | sim1 | sim? |
|--------|---------------|-------------------|------|------|
| vj     | Lj<br>[const] | Lj<br>[coefi<br>] | 0    | 0    |

The algorithm returns sk<sup>y</sup> = (y, isk1, . . . , iskm) as the secret key.

•  $\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{msk},\mathbf{x}\in\mathbb{Z}_p^n)$  generates an IPFE ciphertext ict  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  IPFE. $\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{msk},[\![\mathbf{u}]\!]_1)$  encrypting the vector  $\mathbf{u}$  that contains  $1,\mathbf{x}$ :

| vector | const | $coef_i$        | $sim_1$ | sim <sub>*</sub> |
|--------|-------|-----------------|---------|------------------|
| u      | 1     | $\mathbf{x}[i]$ | 0       | 0                |

It returns ct = (x, ict) as the ciphertext.

• Dec(sk, ct) parses sk as  $(y, isk_1, ..., isk_m)$  and ct as  $(\mathbf{x}, ict)$ , and returns  $\perp$  if  $y(\mathbf{x}) = 0$ . Otherwise, it does the following:

$$\begin{split} \text{for } j \in [m] \colon & & \|\ell_j\|_{\mathcal{T}} \leftarrow \mathsf{IPFE.Dec}(\mathsf{isk}_j, \mathsf{ict}), \\ & \|\mu'\|_{\mathcal{T}} \leftarrow \begin{cases} \frac{1}{f(\mathbf{x})} \mathsf{Eval}(f, \mathbf{x}, [\![\ell_1]\!]_{\mathcal{T}}, \dots, [\![\ell_m]\!]_{\mathcal{T}}), & \text{if } y = f_{\neq 0}; \\ & & \mathsf{Eval}(f, \mathbf{x}, [\![\ell_1]\!]_{\mathcal{T}}, \dots, [\![\ell_m]\!]_{\mathcal{T}}), & \text{if } y = f_{=0}. \end{cases} \end{split}$$

The algorithm returns  $\llbracket \mu' \rrbracket_{\mathrm{T}}$  as the decapsulated pad.

Note: We show the correctness of the scheme. First, by the correctness of IPFE and the definition of vectors  $\mathbf{v}_j$ ,  $\mathbf{u}$ , we have  $\ell_j = \langle \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v}_j \rangle = L_j(\mathbf{x})$  for all  $j \in [m]$ . Next, by the linearity of Eval in  $\ell_1, \ldots, \ell_m$ , we can evaluate the garbling in the exponent of the target group and obtain  $\text{Eval}(f, \mathbf{x}, \ell_1, \ldots, \ell_m) = \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta$  in the exponent. In the two cases where decapsulation should succeed, we have

$$\alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta = \begin{cases} \mu f(\mathbf{x}), & \text{if } y = f_{\neq 0} \text{ and } f(\mathbf{x}) \neq 0; \\ \mu, & \text{if } y = f_{=0} \text{ and } f(\mathbf{x}) = 0. \end{cases}$$

In both cases, the  $\mu'$  above equals to  $\mu$ . Therefore, Dec correctly decapsulates the pad.

<span id="page-32-0"></span>**Theorem 25.** Suppose in Construction 24, the AKGS is piecewise secure and the IPFE scheme is function-hiding, then the constructed 1-ABE scheme is 1-key 1-ciphertext secure.

<u>Proof.</u> Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be any efficient adversary. We want to show that the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in distinguishing  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{1-\mathsf{sk},1-\mathsf{ct}}$  and  $\mathsf{Exp}^1_{1-\mathsf{sk},1-\mathsf{ct}}$  is negligible. Recall that in  $\mathsf{Exp}^b_{1-\mathsf{sk},1-\mathsf{ct}}$  for  $b \in \{0,1\}$ , the adversary is allowed to obtain a single key sk for a policy y with  $\mu^0$  encapsulated, and a single ciphertext ct for an attribute  $\mathbf{x}$ , where the policy y and the attribute  $\mathbf{x}$  are chosen adaptively by  $\mathcal{A}$  and satisfy that either  $y = f_{=0}$  and  $f(\mathbf{x}) \neq 0$ , or  $y = f_{\neq 0}$  and  $f(\mathbf{x}) = 0$ . (If these constraints are not satisfied, the outcome of the experiment is set to 0.) In addition,  $\mathcal{A}$  receives  $\mu^b$  with sk and wants to distinguish the two experiments.

Since the views of  $\mathcal{A}$  in  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{1\mathsf{-sk},1\mathsf{-ct}}$  and  $\mathsf{Exp}^1_{1\mathsf{-sk},1\mathsf{-ct}}$  are identical before  $\mathcal{A}$  receives  $(\mathsf{sk},\mu^b)$ , we can analyze the indistinguishability of these two experiments in two cases:

• Case 1: The ciphertext challenge  $\mathbf{x}$  appears before the secret key query y. In this case, adaptive security collapses down to selective security. This is because by the time the secret key  $\mathsf{sk}$  needs to be generated, the challenge attribute  $\mathbf{x}$  is already known. The proof then proceeds in two simple steps: i) By the function-hiding property of IPFE, we can remove the coefficient vectors  $\mathbf{L}_j$  of the label functions  $L_j$  encoded in  $\mathsf{isk}_j$ , and hardwire directly the labels  $\ell_j = L_j(\mathbf{x})$  in  $\mathsf{isk}_j$ . (Such hardwiring is possible thanks to the fact that  $\mathsf{sk}$  contains many IPFE secret keys, which provide sufficient space for embedding  $\ell_j$ 's.) ii) After replacing  $\mathbf{L}_j$ 's with  $\ell_j$ 's, by the (usual) simulation security of AKGS, the hardwired labels  $\{\ell_j\}_{j\in[m]}$  is independent of  $\mu^0$  whenever decapsulation should fail. Since  $\mu^0$  and  $\mu^1$  are identically distributed, the two hybrids are identical.

• Case 2: The ciphertext challenge  $\mathbf{x}$  appears after the secret key query y. In this case, at the time  $\mathsf{sk}$  needs to be generated, the adversary has not chosen the challenge attribute  $\mathbf{x}$ . As a result, the above argument breaks down at the step of hardwiring: We can no longer hardwire the labels  $\{\ell_j = L_j(\mathbf{x})\}_{j \in [m]}$  in  $\mathsf{sk}$ , as  $\mathbf{x}$  is not yet known. Nor can we hardwire the labels in  $\mathsf{ct}$ , as  $\mathsf{ct}$  contains only a single IPFE ciphertext ict and does not have enough space for embedding  $\{\ell_j\}_{j \in [m]}$ . We resolve this by relying on the piecewise security of AKGS. Roughly speaking, it allows us to gradually switch the coefficient vectors  $\mathbf{L}_j$  of the label functions  $L_j$  to simulated labels  $\ell_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  via a series of hybrids. Between neighboring hybrids, only 2 label functions/labels are changed, which can be hardwired in ict.

Below, we first prove security in the simpler Case 1, and then move to the proof of Case 2. *Proof of Case 1.* Consider the following hybrids:

- <u>Hybrid H</u><sup>b</sup><sub>0</sub> proceeds identically to  $\mathsf{Exp}^b_{1\text{-}\mathsf{sk},1\text{-}\mathsf{ct}}$ , where the vector  $\mathbf{u}$  encrypted in the IPFE ciphertext ict (in the ABE ciphertext ct) and the vectors  $\mathbf{v}_j$  encoded in the IPFE secret keys  $\mathsf{isk}_j$  (in the 1-ABE secret key  $\mathsf{sk}$ ) are described in Figure 1. Note that  $\mathbf{u}$  appears before  $\mathbf{v}_j$  in the experiment.
- <u>Hybrid H</u><sup>b</sup><sub>1</sub> proceeds identically to H<sup>b</sup><sub>0</sub>, except that it sets  $\mathbf{v}_j[\mathsf{const}]$  to the desired inner product of  $\mathbf{v}_j$  with  $\mathbf{u}$  the honest labels  $\ell_j = L_j(\mathbf{x})$  and other positions to 0, as described in Figure 1. Since the inner products  $\langle \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v}_j \rangle$  are the same in H<sup>b</sup><sub>1</sub> and H<sup>b</sup><sub>0</sub>, it follows directly from the function-hiding property of IPFE that these two hybrids are indistinguishable.
- <span id="page-33-0"></span>• <u>Hybrid H</u><sup>b</sup><sub>2</sub> proceeds identically to H<sub>1</sub><sup>b</sup>, except that the hardwired labels  $(\ell_1, \ldots, \ell_m)$  are now simulated using  $Sim(f, \mathbf{x}, \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta)$ . By the usual security of AKGS, H<sup>b</sup><sub>2</sub> and H<sup>b</sup><sub>1</sub> are identically distributed.

| hybrid                                                                                                                    | vector           | const                      | $coef_i$                   | $sim_1$ | $sim_\star$ |  |
|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|------------------|----------------------------|----------------------------|---------|-------------|--|
|                                                                                                                           | u                | 1                          | $\mathbf{x}[i]$            | 0       | 0           |  |
| $H_0^b \equiv Exp_{1-sk,1-ct}^b$                                                                                          | $\mathbf{v}_{j}$ | $\mathbf{L}_{j}[const]$    | $\mathbf{L}_{j}[coef_{i}]$ | 0       | 0           |  |
|                                                                                                                           | u                | 1                          | $\mathbf{x}[i]$            | 0       | 0           |  |
| $H^b_1$                                                                                                                   | $\mathbf{v}_{j}$ | $\ell_j = L_j(\mathbf{x})$ | 0                          | 0       | 0           |  |
| ,                                                                                                                         | u                | 1                          | $\mathbf{x}[i]$            | 0       | 0           |  |
| $H_2^b$                                                                                                                   | $\mathbf{v}_{j}$ | simulated $\ell_j$         | 0                          | 0       | 0           |  |
| Simulation in $H_2^b$ : $(\ell_1,\ldots,\ell_m) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} Sim(f,\mathbf{x},\alpha f(\mathbf{x})+\beta)$ . |                  |                            |                            |         |             |  |

Figure 1: Hybrids in the security proof of 1-ABE for ABPs for the case where the ciphertext challenge comes before the secret key query.

We further argue that  $\mathsf{H}_2^0$  and  $\mathsf{H}_2^1$  are identically distributed. In these hybrids, the labels are simulated as  $\mathsf{Sim}(f,\mathbf{x},\alpha f(\mathbf{x})+\beta)$ . In the two cases where decapsulation should fail, we have

$$\alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta = \begin{cases} 0, & \text{if } y = f_{\neq 0} \text{ and } f(\mathbf{x}) = 0; \\ \eta f(\mathbf{x}) + \mu^0 & (\eta \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p), & \text{if } y = f_{=0} \text{ and } f(\mathbf{x}) \neq 0. \end{cases}$$

In both cases,  $\alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta$  is independent of  $\mu^0$ , hence  $\mu^0$  is a uniform random value independent of everything else in  $\mathsf{H}^0_2$  — so is  $\mu^1$  in  $\mathsf{H}^1_2$ . In addition, everything except  $\mu^0, \mu^1$  are identically distributed in  $\mathsf{H}^0_2$  and  $\mathsf{H}^1_2$ . Therefore, they are identical.

By a hybrid argument, we conclude that  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{1-\mathsf{sk},1-\mathsf{ct}} \approx \mathsf{Exp}^1_{1-\mathsf{sk},1-\mathsf{ct}}$  conditioned on Case 1.

<u>Proof of Case 2.</u> We prove  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{1\text{-sk},1\text{-ct}} \approx \mathsf{Exp}^0_{1\text{-sk},1\text{-ct}}$  conditioned on Case 2 via a series of hybrids. The hybrids are logically divided into 3 groups: i) The first few hybrids remove the first label function, and hardwire the first label that is reversely sampled; ii) The middle hybrids are a loop, and in each iteration one label function is replaced by a simulated label; iii) In the final hybrid, all the labels are simulated, and the encapsulated pad  $\mu^0$  is information-theoretically hidden.

We start with the first few hybrids.

- <u>Hybrid H</u><sup>b</sup><sub>0</sub> proceeds identically to  $\mathsf{Exp}^b_{1\text{-}\mathsf{sk},1\text{-}\mathsf{ct}}$ , where the vector  $\mathbf{u}$  encrypted in the IPFE ciphertext ict (in the ABE ciphertext ct) and the vectors  $\mathbf{v}_j$  encoded in the IPFE secret keys  $\mathsf{isk}_j$  (in the ABE secret key  $\mathsf{sk}$ ) are described in Figure 2. Different from Case 1, the vector  $\mathbf{u}$  appears after  $\mathbf{v}_j$  in the experiment.
- <u>Hybrid</u>  $H_1^b$  proceeds identically to  $H_0^b$ , except that it removes the coefficients of the first label function  $\mathbf{L}_1$  from  $\mathbf{v}_1[\mathsf{const}], \mathbf{v}_1[\mathsf{coef}_i]$ , and hardwires the honest first label  $\ell_1 = L_1(\mathbf{x})$  in  $\mathbf{u}[\mathsf{sim}_1]$  as described in Figure 2. Since the inner product remains the same as in  $H_0^b$ , by the function-hiding property of IPFE, we have that  $H_1^b$  and  $H_0^b$  are indistinguishable.
- <u>Hybrid  $H_2^b$ </u> proceeds identically to  $H_1^b$ , except that the hardwired first label is now reversely sampled as

$$\ell_1 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{RevSamp}(f, \mathbf{x}, \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta, \ell_2, \dots, \ell_m),$$

where  $\ell_j = L_j(\mathbf{x})$  for j > 1 are the honest labels. By the reverse sampleability of AKGS (Definition 16),  $\mathsf{H}_2^b$  and  $\mathsf{H}_1^b$  are identically distributed.

<u>Hybrid</u>  $H_4^b$  is our final hybrid, which proceeds identically to  $H_2^b$ , except that for all j > 1, the coefficient vector  $\mathbf{L}_j$  of the label function  $L_j$  is removed from  $\mathbf{v}_j$ , and a simulated label  $\ell_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  is hardwired in  $\mathbf{v}_j[\mathsf{const}]$ , as depicted in Figure 2. Correspondingly, the first label  $\ell_1$  is reversely sampled using these simulated labels. The hybrid is similar to  $H_2^b$  in Case 1, except for the location where  $\ell_1$  is embedded. However, we cannot move from  $H_2^b$  to  $H_4^b$  in one shot. Instead, we go through a loop consisting of  $H_{3,j,1}^b, \ldots, H_{3,j,4}^b$  for  $1 < j \le m$ :

- <u>Hybrid H</u><sup>b</sup><sub>3,j,1</sub> proceeds identically to H<sup>b</sup><sub>2</sub>, except that for all j' s.t. 1 < j' < j, the coefficient vector  $\mathbf{L}_{j'}$  of the label function  $L_{j'}$  is removed from  $\mathbf{v}_{j'}$ , and a simulated label  $\ell_{j'} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  is hardwired in  $\mathbf{v}_{j'}[\mathsf{const}]$ , as described in Figure 3. In H<sup>b</sup><sub>3,j,1</sub>,  $\ell_1$  is reversely sampled using simulated  $\ell_2, \ldots, \ell_{j-1}$  and honest  $\ell_j, \ldots, \ell_m$ .
- <u>Hybrid</u>  $H_{3,j,2}^b$  proceeds identically to  $H_{3,j,1}^b$ , except that it removes the coefficient vector of the  $j^{th}$  label function  $L_j$  from  $\mathbf{v}_j$ , and hardwires the honest  $j^{th}$  label  $\ell_j = L_j(\mathbf{x})$  in  $\mathbf{u}[\mathsf{sim}_{\star}]$ . Note that  $\mathbf{L}_j$  is gone in  $H_{3,j,2}^b$  and the honest label  $\ell_j$  is left. The modified vectors  $\mathbf{v}_j$  and  $\mathbf{u}$  are described in Figure 3. Since the inner products remain the same, by the function-hiding property of IPFE,  $H_{3,j,2}^b$  and  $H_{3,j,1}^b$  are indistinguishable.
- <u>Hybrid  $H_{3,j,3}^b$ </u> proceeds identically to  $H_{3,j,2}^b$ , except that the  $j^{\text{th}}$  label is simulated as  $\ell_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ . Note that in hybrids  $H_{3,j,2}^b$  and  $H_{3,j,3}^b$ , only the coefficient vectors  $\mathbf{L}_{j'}$  of the label functions

<span id="page-35-0"></span>

|------------------------------------------------|--------------------------|-------------------------------------------------|----------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------|------------------|
|                                                | $\mathbf{v}_1$           | $\mathbf{L}_1[const]$                           | $\mathbf{L}_1[coef_i]$     | $\llbracket 0 \rrbracket$                                                         | 0                |
| $H_0^b \equiv Exp_{1\text{-}sk,1\text{-}ct}^b$ | $j > 1$ : $\mathbf{v}_j$ | $\mathbf{L}_{j}[const]$                         | $\mathbf{L}_{j}[coef_{i}]$ | 0                                                                                 | 0                |

|                                                | u                        | 1                                               | $\mathbf{x}[i]$            | $\ell_1 \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\scriptscriptstyle\$}{\leftarrow} RevSamp(\cdots)$ | 0                |

Reverse sampling in  $\mathsf{H}_2^b, \mathsf{H}_4^b$ :  $\ell_1 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{RevSamp}(f, \mathbf{x}, \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta, \ell_2, \dots, \ell_m)$ . In  $\mathsf{H}_2^b$ , the labels  $\ell_j$  (j > 1) are computed honestly as  $L_j(\mathbf{x})$  using IPFE. In  $\mathsf{H}_4^b$ , they are simulated as random and hardwired in  $\mathsf{sk}$ .

|                 | j'>j:       | $\mathbf{v}_{j'}$ | $\mathbf{L}_{j'}[const]$                                                               | $\mathbf{L}_{j'}[coef_i]$  | 0        | 0                                               |
|                 |             | u                 | 1                                                                                      | $\mathbf{x}[i]$            | $\ell_1$ | $\ell_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ |
|                 |             | $\mathbf{v}_1$    | 0                                                                                      | 0                          | 1        | 0                                               |
| $H^b_{3,j,4}$   | 1 < j' < j: | $\mathbf{v}_{j'}$ | $\ell_{j'} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$                                     | 0                          | 0        | 0                                               |
| =               |             | $\mathbf{v}_{j}$  | $\boxed{\ell_j \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\scriptscriptstyle\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p}$ | 0                          | 0        | 0                                               |
| $H^b_{3,j+1,1}$ | j'>j:       | $\mathbf{v}_{j'}$ | $\mathbf{L}_{j'}[const]$                                                               | $\mathbf{L}_{j'}[coef_i]$  | 0        | 0                                               |
|                 |             | u                 | 1                                                                                      | $\mathbf{x}[i]$            | $\ell_1$ | 0                                               |

In this iteration, the labels  $\ell_{j'}$  are...

j' = 1: simulated as RevSamp $(f, \mathbf{x}, \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta, \ell_2, \dots, \ell_m)$ and hardwired in  $\mathsf{ct}$ 

1 < j' < j: simulated as random and hardwired in sk

j'=j: computed as  $L_j(\mathbf{x})$  using IPFE  $\rightarrow$  computed as  $L_j(\mathbf{x})$  and hardwired in ct

 $\rightarrow$  simulated as random and hardwired in ct

 $\rightarrow$  simulated as random and hardwired in  ${\sf sk}$ 

j' > j: computed as  $L_{j'}(\mathbf{x})$  using IPFE

Figure 3: The loop hybrids in the security proof of 1-ABE for ABPs for the case where the ciphertext challenge comes after the secret key query.

 $L_{j'}$  for j' > j appear. For 1 < j' < j, a simulated label  $\ell_{j'}$  is hardwired in  $\mathbf{v}_{j'}[\mathsf{const}]$ . In both hybrids, the first label  $\ell_1$  can be efficiently computed from  $\ell_2, \ldots, \ell_{j-1}$  (simulated),  $\ell_j$  (honest or simulated) and  $\mathbf{L}_{j+1}, \ldots, \mathbf{L}_m$  (producing honest labels). Therefore, by the marginal randomness property of AKGS (Definition 16),  $\mathsf{H}_{3,j,3}^b$  and  $\mathsf{H}_{3,j,2}^b$  are identically distributed.

• <u>Hybrid  $H_{3,j,4}^b$ </u> proceeds identically to  $H_{3,j,3}^b$ , except that the simulated  $j^{\text{th}}$  label  $\ell_j$  is moved from  $\mathbf{u}[\mathsf{sim}_{\star}]$  to  $\mathbf{v}_j[\mathsf{const}]$  as described in Figure 3 (so that  $\mathbf{u}[\mathsf{sim}_{\star}]$  is free to be used in the next iteration of hybrids  $H_{3,j+1,1}^b$  to  $H_{3,j+1,4}^b$ ). Since the inner products remain the same, by the function-hiding property of IPFE,  $H_{3,j,4}^b$  and  $H_{3,j,3}^b$  are indistinguishable. In addition, observe that  $H_{3,j,4}^b \equiv H_{3,j+1,1}^b$ .

Moreover,  $\mathsf{H}_2^b \equiv \mathsf{H}_{3,2,1}^b$  and  $\mathsf{H}_4^b \equiv \mathsf{H}_{3,m,4}^b$ . Thus, by a hybrid argument, we have  $\mathsf{H}_2^b \approx \mathsf{H}_4^b$ . We further argue that  $\mathsf{H}_4^0$  and  $\mathsf{H}_4^1$  are identically distributed. Observe that in these hybrids, the labels are simulated as

$$\begin{split} &\ell_{j'} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p \quad \text{for } j' = 2, \dots, m, \\ &\ell_1 \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{RevSamp}(f, \mathbf{x}, \alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta, \ell_2, \dots, \ell_m). \end{split}$$

In the two cases where decapsulation should fail, we have

$$\alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta = \begin{cases} 0, & \text{if } y = f_{\neq 0} \text{ and } f(\mathbf{x}) = 0; \\ \eta f(\mathbf{x}) + \mu^0 & (\eta \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p), & \text{if } y = f_{=0} \text{ and } f(\mathbf{x}) \neq 0. \end{cases}$$

In both cases,  $\alpha f(\mathbf{x}) + \beta$  is independent of  $\mu^0$ , hence  $\mu^0$  is a uniform random value independent of everything else in  $\mathsf{H}_4^0$  — so is  $\mu^1$  in  $\mathsf{H}_4^1$ . In addition, everything except  $\mu^0, \mu^1$  are identically distributed in  $\mathsf{H}_4^0$  and  $\mathsf{H}_4^1$ . Therefore, they are identical.

By a hybrid argument,  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{1\text{-sk},1\text{-ct}} \approx \mathsf{Exp}^1_{1\text{-sk},1\text{-ct}}$  conditioned on Case 2.

### <span id="page-37-0"></span>6.3 KP-ABE for ABPs

We now lift the 1-ABE scheme to a full-fledged ABE by implementing the ideas discussed in Section 2.2.

<span id="page-37-1"></span>Construction 26 (KP-ABE). We describe the construction for any fixed value of the security parameter  $\lambda$  and suppress the appearance of  $\lambda$  below for simplicity of notations. Let (Garble, Eval) be an AKGS for a function class  $\mathcal{F}$ ,  $\mathcal{G}$  pairing groups of order p such that MDDH<sub>k</sub> holds in  $G_2$ , and (IPFE.Setup, IPFE.KeyGen, IPFE.Enc, IPFE.Dec) a slotted IPFE based on  $\mathcal{G}$ . We construct an ABE scheme for message space  $M = G_T$ , the target group of the pairing, and the predicate space  $\mathcal{P}$  induced by  $\mathcal{F}$ :

$$X_n = \mathbb{Z}_p^n, \quad Y_n = \{ f_{\neq 0}, f_{=0} \mid f \in \mathcal{F}, f : \mathbb{Z}_p^n \to \mathbb{Z}_p \},$$
  
$$\mathcal{P} = \{ P_n : X_n \times Y_n \to \{0, 1\}, (\mathbf{x}, y) \mapsto y(\mathbf{x}) \mid n \in \mathbb{N} \}.$$

The ABE scheme (Setup, KeyGen, Enc, Dec) operates as follows:

• Setup(1<sup>n</sup>) takes the attribute length in unary (i.e.,  $P_n$  is encoded as 1<sup>n</sup>) as input. It generates IPFE master public/secret key pair (msk, mpk)  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  IPFE.Setup( $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}}, \mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}}$ ) for the following slots:

$$\begin{split} & \boldsymbol{\mathfrak{S}}_{\mathrm{pub}} = \{\mathsf{pad}, \mathsf{const}^t, \mathsf{coef}_i^t \,|\, t \in [k], i \in [n]\}, \\ & \boldsymbol{\mathfrak{S}}_{\mathrm{priv}} = \{\mathsf{const}, \mathsf{coef}_i, \mathsf{sim}_1, \mathsf{sim}_\star \,|\, i \in [n]\} \,(= \boldsymbol{\mathfrak{S}}_{\text{1-ABE}}). \end{split}$$

It returns (mpk, msk) as the master public/secret key pair.

Note: We explain the names of the indices. For each  $t \in [k]$ , the positions indexed by  $\mathsf{const}^t$ ,  $\mathsf{coef}_1^t, \ldots, \mathsf{coef}_n^t$  encode the coefficient vectors  $\mathbf{L}_j^t$  of the label functions of one garbling in the secret key, and encrypt a random multiple of  $(1, \mathbf{x})$  in the ciphertext. The position indexed by  $\mathsf{pad}$  encodes 1 in the secret key and encrypts a random pad  $h \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  in the ciphertext. The private slot is reserved for the security proof and values at those indices are set to 0 by the honest algorithms.

• KeyGen(msk,  $y \in Y_n$ ) samples  $\mu \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ ,  $\eta \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  and creates k garblings of the function f underlying y as follows:

$$\begin{cases} \boldsymbol{\alpha} \leftarrow \boldsymbol{\mu}, & \boldsymbol{\beta} \leftarrow \mathbf{0}, & \text{if } y = f_{\neq 0}; \\ \boldsymbol{\alpha} \leftarrow \boldsymbol{\eta}, & \boldsymbol{\beta} \leftarrow \boldsymbol{\mu}, & \text{if } y = f_{=0}; \end{cases}$$
 for  $t \in [k]$ :  $(\mathbf{L}_1^t, \dots, \mathbf{L}_m^t) \leftarrow \mathsf{Garble}(f, \boldsymbol{\alpha}[t], \boldsymbol{\beta}[t]; \mathbf{r}_t)$ .

The randomness vectors  $\mathbf{r}_1, \dots, \mathbf{r}_k$  for garbling f are independently sampled and written explicitly. It sets the vector  $\mathbf{v}_{\text{pad}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathfrak{s}}$  to encode the random pads  $\boldsymbol{\mu}$ , and the vector  $\mathbf{v}_j \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathfrak{s}}$  for  $j \in [m]$  to encode the  $j^{\text{th}}$  label function in all the k instances of garbling:

| vector                                                                    | pad                                    | $const^t$                                         | $coef_i^t$                         | in $\mathfrak{s}_{\mathrm{priv}}$ |
|---------------------------------------------------------------------------|----------------------------------------|---------------------------------------------------|------------------------------------|-----------------------------------|
| $\begin{array}{c} \mathbf{v}_{\mathrm{pad}} \ \mathbf{v}_{j} \end{array}$ | $\begin{vmatrix} 1 \\ 0 \end{vmatrix}$ | $\boldsymbol{\mu}[t] \ \mathbf{L}_{j}^{t}[const]$ | $0 \ \mathbf{L}_{j}^{t}[coef_{i}]$ | 0                                 |

The algorithm then generates IPFE secret keys for them:

$$\begin{aligned} \mathsf{isk}_{\mathrm{pad}} & \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{IPFE}.\mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, [\![\mathbf{v}_{\mathrm{pad}}]\!]_2), \\ \mathsf{for} \ j \in [m] \colon \ \mathsf{isk}_j & \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{IPFE}.\mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, [\![\mathbf{v}_j]\!]_2). \end{aligned}$$

It returns  $sk = (isk_{pad}, y, isk_1, \dots, isk_m)$ .

• Enc(mpk,  $\mathbf{x} \in X_n, g \in M$ ) samples a random pad  $h \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  and random multipliers  $\mathbf{s} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , and sets the vector  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}}}$  to encode them as follows:

| vector | pad           | $const^t$       | $coef_i^t$                   |
|--------|---------------|-----------------|------------------------------|
| u      | $\mid h \mid$ | $\mathbf{s}[t]$ | $\mathbf{s}[t]\mathbf{x}[i]$ |

The algorithm generates an IPFE ciphertext ict  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  IPFE.SlotEnc(mpk,  $\llbracket \mathbf{u} \rrbracket_1$ ) and returns ct =  $(g + \llbracket h \rrbracket_T, \mathbf{x}, \text{ict})$ .

• Dec(sk, ct) parses sk as  $(\mathsf{isk}_{pad}, y, \mathsf{isk}_1, \dots, \mathsf{isk}_m)$  and ct as  $(\llbracket z \rrbracket_T, \mathbf{x}, \mathsf{ict})$ . It returns  $\bot$  if  $y(\mathbf{x}) = 0$ . Otherwise, it does the following:

$$\begin{split} & \|z'\|_{\mathrm{T}} \leftarrow \mathsf{IPFE.Dec}(\mathsf{isk}_{\mathrm{pad}}, \mathsf{ict}), \\ & \text{for } j \in [m] \colon \ \|\ell_j\|_{\mathrm{T}} \leftarrow \mathsf{IPFE.Dec}(\mathsf{isk}_j, \mathsf{ict}), \\ & \|\mu'\|_{\mathrm{T}} \leftarrow \begin{cases} \frac{1}{f(\mathbf{x})} \mathsf{Eval}(f, \mathbf{x}, [\![\ell_1]\!]_{\mathrm{T}}, \dots, [\![\ell_m]\!]_{\mathrm{T}}), & \text{if } y = f_{\neq 0}; \\ & \mathsf{Eval}(f, \mathbf{x}, [\![\ell_1]\!]_{\mathrm{T}}, \dots, [\![\ell_m]\!]_{\mathrm{T}}), & \text{if } y = f_{=0}. \end{cases} \end{split}$$

The algorithm returns  $[\![z]\!]_T + [\![\mu']\!]_T - [\![z']\!]_T$  as the decrypted message.

Note: We show the correctness of the scheme. Consider the random linear combination  $\overline{L}_j$  of label functions  $L_j^1, \ldots, L_j^k$  with weights  $\mathbf{s}$ :

for 
$$j \in [m]$$
:  $\overline{L}_j(\mathbf{x}) = \sum_{t \in [k]} \mathbf{s}[t] L_j^t(\mathbf{x}), \quad \overline{\mathbf{L}}_j = \sum_{t \in [k]} \mathbf{s}[t] \mathbf{L}_j^t.$ 

The coefficient vector of  $\overline{\mathbf{L}}_j$  is exactly  $\overline{\mathbf{L}}_j$  defined above. Furthermore, by the linearity of Garble, we know  $(\overline{\mathbf{L}}_1,\ldots,\overline{\mathbf{L}}_m)=\mathsf{Garble}(f,\overline{\alpha},\overline{\beta};\overline{\mathbf{r}}),$  where  $\overline{\alpha},\overline{\beta},$  and  $\overline{\mathbf{r}}$  are the random linear combinations (with the same weights  $\mathbf{s}$ ) of the components of  $\alpha$ , the components of  $\beta$ , and the randomness vectors  $\{\mathbf{r}_t\}_{t\in[k]}$  in the k instances of garbling, i.e.,

$$\overline{\alpha} = \langle \mathbf{s}, \boldsymbol{\alpha} \rangle, \quad \overline{\beta} = \langle \mathbf{s}, \boldsymbol{\beta} \rangle, \quad \overline{\mathbf{r}} = \sum_{t \in [k]} \mathbf{s}[t] \mathbf{r}_t.$$

Observe that by the definition of  $\mathbf{v}_{pad}$ ,  $\mathbf{v}_{j}$ ,  $\mathbf{u}$  and the correctness of the IPFE scheme,

$$z' = \langle \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v}_{\text{pad}} \rangle = h + \langle \mathbf{s}, \boldsymbol{\mu} \rangle = h + \overline{\mu},$$
 for  $j \in [m]$ :  $\ell_j = \langle \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v}_j \rangle = \sum_{t \in [k]} \mathbf{s}[t] L_j^t(\mathbf{x}) = \overline{L}_j(\mathbf{x}),$ 

where  $\overline{\mu} = \langle \mathbf{s}, \boldsymbol{\mu} \rangle$  is the random linear combination of the components of  $\boldsymbol{\mu}$  with weights  $\mathbf{s}$ . By the linearity of Eval in  $\ell_1, \ldots, \ell_m$ , we can evaluate the garbling in the exponent of the target group and obtain  $\text{Eval}(f, \mathbf{x}, \ell_1, \ldots, \ell_m) = \overline{\alpha} f(\mathbf{x}) + \overline{\beta}$  in the exponent. In the two cases where decryption should succeed, we have

$$\overline{\alpha}f(\mathbf{x}) + \overline{\beta} = \begin{cases} \overline{\alpha}f(\mathbf{x}) = \overline{\mu}f(\mathbf{x}), & \text{if } y = f_{\neq 0} \text{ and } f(\mathbf{x}) \neq 0; \\ \overline{\beta} = \overline{\mu}, & \text{if } y = f_{=0} \text{ and } f(\mathbf{x}) = 0. \end{cases}$$

In both cases, we have  $\mu' = \overline{\mu}$ , hence  $[\![z]\!]_T + [\![\mu']\!]_T - [\![z']\!]_T = g + [\![h]\!]_T + [\![\overline{\mu}]\!]_T - [\![h + \overline{\mu}]\!]_T = g$ , i.e., Dec correctly recovers the message.

<span id="page-39-0"></span>**Theorem 27.** Suppose in Construction 26, the AKGS is piecewise secure, the MDDH<sub>k</sub> assumption holds in  $G_2$ , and the slotted IPFE is function-hiding, then the construction is a secure ABE scheme.

<u>Proof.</u> Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be any efficient adversary. We show that the distinguishing advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  against  $\mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{CPA}}^0$  and  $\mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{CPA}}^1$  is negligible. Recall that in  $\mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{CPA}}^b$ , the adversary can receive many secret keys  $\mathsf{sk}_q$  for different policies  $y_q$  and a ciphertext ct for attribute  $\mathbf{x}$  and message  $g_b$ , where  $\{y_q\}_{q\in[Q]}$  and  $(\mathbf{x},g_0,g_1)$  are chosen adaptively by  $\mathcal{A}$ , subject to the constraint that no individual key should decrypt the challenge ciphertext, i.e.,  $P(\mathbf{x},y_q)=0$  for all  $q\in[Q]$ . (The output of the experiment is set to 0, if the constraint is not satisfied.)

We start with the high-level ideas of the proof. Recall that by construction, ct and  $\mathsf{sk}_q$  contain respectively a slotted IPFE ciphertext ict and many IPFE secret keys  $\mathsf{isk}_{q,\mathrm{pad}}$ ,  $\{\mathsf{isk}_{q,j}\}_{j\in[m_q]}$  such that decryption computes in the exponent i) a garbling  $\overline{L}_q(\mathbf{x})$  that reveals a random pad  $\overline{\mu}_q$  from the evaluation result  $\overline{\alpha}_q f_q(x) + \overline{\beta}_q$  if the policy is satisfied  $(P(\mathbf{x}, y_q) = 1)$ , and ii) a sum  $h + \overline{\mu}_q$ . Revealing any  $[\![\overline{\mu}_q]\!]_T$  allows one to recover the message  $g_b$  from  $g_b + [\![h]\!]_T$  in ct. Therefore, to show  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{\mathrm{CPA}} \approx \mathsf{Exp}^1_{\mathrm{CPA}}$ , we want to argue that when no policy is satisfied, all the pads  $\overline{\mu}_q$  are pseudorandom and hide h.

To do so, the proof proceeds in three steps via a sequence of hybrids. In the first step — hybrids  $\mathsf{H}_0^b \equiv \mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{CPA}}^b$  to  $\mathsf{H}_3^b$  — we embed in the *private* slot of ict the vector  $(1,\mathbf{x})$ , in the *private* slot of isk<sub>q,pad</sub> randomly and independently sampled pads  $\widehat{\mu}_q$ , and in the *private* slot of isk<sub>q,j</sub> randomly and independently generated label functions  $\widehat{L}_{q,j}$  embedding  $\widehat{\mu}_q$ . In the second step — hybrids  $\mathsf{H}_{4,1}^b \equiv \mathsf{H}_3^b$  to  $\mathsf{H}_{4,Q+1}^b \equiv \mathsf{H}_5^b$  — we observe that the *private* slots of ict and isk<sub>q,j</sub> match exactly the ciphertext and secret keys of our 1-ABE scheme (i.e., they encode the same vectors and have the function-hiding property). Therefore, using the same argument as the security proof of 1-ABE, we can switch the random pads  $\widehat{\mu}_q$  encoded in isk<sub>q,pad</sub> to independent random values  $\widehat{\mu}_q'$ . Lastly, in the third step —  $\mathsf{H}_5^b$  to  $\mathsf{H}_6^b$  — we remove h from ict and  $\widehat{\mu}_q'$  from isk<sub>q,pad</sub>, and embed  $h + \widehat{\mu}_q'$  in sk<sub>q,pad</sub>. This allows us to argue that h is hidden by  $\widehat{\mu}_q'$ , and the message  $g_b$  is also hidden.

Next, we proceed to describing the hybrids formally.

- <u>Hybrid H</u><sup>b</sup><sub>0</sub> proceeds identically to  $\mathsf{Exp}^b_{\mathsf{CPA}}$ , where the vector  $\mathbf{u}$  encrypted in the IPFE ciphertext ict (in ct), the vectors  $\mathbf{v}_{q,j}$  encoded in the IPFE secret keys  $\mathsf{isk}_{q,j}$  (in  $\mathsf{sk}_q$ ), and the vectors  $\mathbf{v}_{q,\mathsf{pad}}$  in  $\mathsf{isk}_{q,\mathsf{pad}}$  (in  $\mathsf{sk}_q$ ) are described in Figure 4.
