| $G_5^\beta$                                | $\mathbf{v}_{\varphi,\text{init}},\mathbf{v}_{\varphi,q}$                                                                                        | $\mu, \mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{f}}$ 's and $h$                                                                                                                               | 1                                                                                                                  | 0                                                                                                         | $\boxed{1}$                                                            | $\propto (\widehat{s}\widehat{\mu}_{\varphi}^0, \widehat{s}\widehat{\mathbf{r}}_{\varphi,\mathrm{f}})$ |
|                                            | $\mathbf{u}_{\text{pad}}$ $\mathbf{u}_{\text{init}}, \mathbf{u}_{t,i,j,\mathbf{W}}$                                                              |                                                                                                                                                                         | 1                                                                                                                  | $\propto (1, \mathbf{r}_{\mathrm{x}})$                                                                    |                                                                        | $\propto (1, \widehat{\mathbf{r}}_{\mathrm{x}})$                                                       |
|                                            | $\mathbf{v}_{\varphi,\mathrm{pad}}$                                                                                                              |                                                                                                                                                                         | 0                                                                                                                  |                                                                                                           | $\widehat{\mu}_{\varphi}^{\beta}$                                      |                                                                                                        |
| $G_6^\beta$                                | $\mathbf{v}_{\varphi,\text{init}},\mathbf{v}_{\varphi,q}$                                                                                        | $\mu, \mathbf{r}_{\mathrm{f}}$ 's                                                                                                                                       |                                                                                                                    | 0                                                                                                         |                                                                        | $\propto (\widehat{\mu}_{\varphi}^0, \widehat{\mathbf{r}}_{\varphi,\mathrm{f}})$                       |
| G <sub>6</sub>                             | $\mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{pad}}$                                                                                                                      | and $h$                                                                                                                                                                 | 1                                                                                                                  |                                                                                                           | 1                                                                      |                                                                                                        |
|                                            | $\mathbf{u}_{\text{init}}, \mathbf{u}_{t,i,j,\mathbf{W}}$                                                                                        |                                                                                                                                                                         |                                                                                                                    | $\propto (1, \mathbf{r}_{\mathrm{x}})$                                                                    |                                                                        | $\propto (1, \widehat{\mathbf{r}}_x)$                                                                  |

For brevity, the vectors for computing the labels are not spelled out, and the secret key vectors other than those for  $\mathsf{sk}_\varphi$  are suppressed except in  $\mathsf{G}_0^\beta$ . See Figure 10 for the meaning of " $\propto \mathbf{z}$ ".

Figure 11: The hybrids for showing  $\mathsf{H}^b_{4,\varphi} \approx \mathsf{H}^b_{4,\varphi+1}$  in the proof of IND-CPA security of our KP-ABE scheme for  $\mathsf{L}$ .

• <u>Hybrid  $G_4^{\beta}$ </u> proceeds identically to  $G_3^{\beta}$ , except that in the ciphertext vectors, the  $\hat{\mathbf{s}}$  in  $\mathfrak{s}_{1\text{-ABE}}$  is replaced by  $\hat{\mathbf{s}}$   $\hat{\mathbf{r}}_{x}$ . The indistinguishability between  $G_3^{\beta}$  and  $G_4^{\beta}$  again follows by the DDH assumption in  $G_1$ :

$$[\![\widehat{s},\widehat{\mathbf{s}}]\!]_1 \approx [\![\widehat{s},\widehat{s}\,\widehat{\mathbf{r}}_{\mathbf{x}}]\!]_1 \text{ for } \widehat{\mathbf{s}},\widehat{\mathbf{r}}_{\mathbf{x}} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{[0..T]\times[N]\times[S]\times\{0,1\}^S}, \widehat{s} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p.$$

- <u>Hybrid</u>  $G_{5}^{\beta}$  proceeds identically to  $G_{4}^{\beta}$ , except that the multiplier  $\hat{s}$  is moved from **u**'s back to  $\mathbf{v}_{\varphi}$ 's as depicted in Figure 11. The indistinguishability between the two hybrids follows by the function-hiding property of IPFE.
- <u>Hybrid</u>  $\mathsf{G}_{6}^{\beta}$  proceeds identically to  $\mathsf{G}_{5}^{\beta}$ , except that the multiplier  $\widehat{s}$  is removed. We have  $\mathsf{G}_{6}^{\beta} \approx_{s} \mathsf{G}_{5}^{\beta}$ .

Lastly, observe that in  $\mathsf{G}_6^\beta$ , only the ciphertext vectors and the vectors for the  $\varphi^{\mathrm{th}}$  secret key have non-zero values in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$ , and those values are generated exactly as specified by the 1-ABE for L (Construction 33). We use syntactically the same proof of the security of the 1-ABE scheme for L (Theorem 34) to argue  $\mathsf{G}_6^0 \approx \mathsf{G}_6^1$ :

- Each step in the proof of Theorem 34 uses the function-hiding property, the DDH assumption or the special piecewise security, all of which are still valid when translated into the case of  $\mathsf{G}_6^0$  and  $\mathsf{G}_6^1$  in the only natural way. More specifically, i) the function-hiding property is unaffected by the public slot,  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{copy}}$ , pad<sup>copy</sup> and the additional vectors (in the other secret keys), as the values being manipulated during the proof are all in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$  in the private slot, and the additional vectors (having values 0 in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$ ) cannot "detect" the changes in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$ ; and ii) the applications of the DDH assumptions and the special piecewise security still go through, because the garbling in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$  is generated using randomness independent of everything else in  $\mathsf{G}_6^\beta$ .
- In the translated version of the proof of Theorem 34,  $\mathbf{v}_{\varphi,\mathrm{pad}}[\mathsf{pad}^{\mathsf{temp}}]$  is always set to  $\widehat{\mu}_{\varphi}^{\beta}$ . In the final hybrids, both  $\widehat{\mu}_{\varphi}^{0}$  and  $\widehat{\mu}_{\varphi}^{1}$  are uniformly random and independent of everything else, so they can replace each other.

Therefore, 
$$\mathsf{G}_6^0 \approx \mathsf{G}_6^1$$
, and by a hybrid argument, we conclude  $\mathsf{H}_{4,\varphi}^b \equiv \mathsf{G}_0^0 \approx \mathsf{G}_0^1 \equiv \mathsf{H}_{4,\varphi+1}^b$ .

<u>Remarks.</u> The natural way to generalize Construction 37 to MDDH<sub>k</sub> is to let the public slot store k independent garblings and let the private slot provide space for k+1 garblings — among the k+1 copies in the private slot, k of them are the counterpart of  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{copy}}$ , and the last one of them is the counterpart of  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$ . Of course, the underlying 1-ABE scheme must also be based on MDDH<sub>k</sub>. This natural generalization uses vectors of dimension  $\Theta(k^2)$ , because each copy of the garbling already needs  $\Theta(k)$  indices.

A simple optimization that shortens the vectors to only  $\Theta(k)$  is to realize that the rerandomization of the (half) garblings in the security proof of ABE can be done without creating k copies of 1-ABE, essentially because each 1-ABE already uses k copies of random values to jointly generate the label functions. For this optimization to work, the copy in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}}$  needs to embed k independent pads in the secret keys and k independent random multipliers in the ciphertext, and decapsulation gives the inner product of the two. For the other two "compartments",  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{copy}}$  and  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$ , each only needs to provide space for one pad.

The ABE scheme still uses 3 copies, and we use  $(\mathbf{X} \parallel \mathbf{Y} \parallel \mathbf{Z})$  to represent an ABE key/ciphertext generated with randomness  $\mathbf{X}, \mathbf{Y}, \mathbf{Z}$  for the  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}}, \mathfrak{s}_{\text{copy}}, \mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$  copies (0 means no copy is there; pads

are omitted). Roughly speaking, the first step (isolating the interaction between sk's and ct from the other ciphertexts) is done as follows:

$$\begin{array}{c|ccccccccccccccccccccccccccccccccccc$$

Here,  $\mathbf{R}_{\mathbf{f},\varphi}$ ,  $\widehat{\mathbf{R}}_{\mathbf{f},\varphi}$   $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times Q_{\varphi}}$ ,  $\mathbf{R}_{\mathbf{x}}$   $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{[k] \times ([0..T] \times [N] \times [S] \times \{0,1\}^S)}$  and  $\mathbf{A}$   $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times k}$ . The first transition is a change of variable introducing only a negligible statistical error. The second one is due to the function-hiding property of IPFE. The third reduces to  $\mathrm{MDDH}_{k,\Phi+Q_1+\dots+Q_{\Phi}}^k$ .

The second step is to change the pads in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{copy}}$  to be inconsistent one by one. Similar to the proof of Claim 39, we achieve this via a loop of  $\Phi$  iterations, one for each secret key. In the  $\varphi^{\text{th}}$  iteration, we i) introduce a random multiplier (this time a  $k \times k$  matrix) in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{copy}}$  of  $\mathsf{sk}_{\varphi}$ , ii) isolate the interaction between  $\mathsf{ct}$  and  $\mathsf{sk}_{\varphi}$  into  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$  with the multiplier moved into  $\mathsf{ct}$ , iii) switch the randomness in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{1-ABE}}$  of  $\mathsf{ct}$  to be sampled independently from that in  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{copy}}$  in  $\mathsf{ct}$ , and iv) invoke 1-ABE security.

The last step is to remove the ciphertext pad from ct and hide it using the inconsistent pads in sk's, which is straightforward.

#### <span id="page-73-0"></span>7.4 Extension to NL

Applying our construction of KP-ABE for L to non-deterministic Turing machines yields a KP-ABE for NL. In this section, we discuss what modifications need to be made to handle NL.

**Non-Deterministic Turing Machines.** The definitions of non-deterministic Turing machines (NTMs) and time/space bounded computation are the same as Definition 12, except with these changes:

- The transition criterion  $\delta$  can be any relation between (i.e., any subset of the Cartesian product of)  $[Q] \times \{0,1\}^2$  and  $[Q] \times \{0,1\} \times \{0,\pm 1\}^2$ , where  $((q,x,w),(q',w',\Delta i,\Delta j)) \in \delta$  means that if the current state is q, the input tape symbol under scan is x and the work tape symbol under scan is w, then it is valid to transit into state q', overwrite w with w', and move the input and work tape pointer by offsets  $\Delta i$  and  $\Delta j$  respectively.
- The definition of hanging in accepting states is that for all  $q \in [Q]$  such that  $\mathbf{y}_{acc}[q] = 1$  and all  $x, w \in \{0, 1\}$ ,

$$\delta \cap \left( \{ (q, x, w) \} \times ([Q] \times \{0, 1\} \times \{0, \pm 1\}^2) \right) = \left\{ \left( (q, x, w), (q, w, 0, 0) \right) \right\}.$$

• In the definition of acceptance,

$$\begin{split} \delta(q_t,\mathbf{x}[i_t],\mathbf{W}_t[j_t]) &= (q_{t+1},\mathbf{W}_{t+1}[j_t],i_{t+1}-i_t,j_{t+1}-j_t)\\ \text{is changed to} \quad \left((q_t,\mathbf{x}[i_t],\mathbf{W}_t[j_t]),(q_{t+1},\mathbf{W}_{t+1}[j_t],i_{t+1}-i_t,j_{t+1}-j_t)\right) \in \delta. \end{split}$$

Note that in the case of non-deterministic machines, the machine might move off the tapes with certain choices of transitions, but that does not necessarily lead to rejection — it is just that particular path that is silently dropped. As long as one valid path does not exceed the space bound and land in an accepting state after T steps, the input is accepted.

Arithmetizing NTM Computation. The same matrix multiplication formula (thus the same AKGS) works for non-deterministic computation. We just need to use the non-deterministic version of the transition matrix, which share the same block structure as the deterministic ones.

Let M = (Q, yacc, δ) be an NTM. The transition blocks of M are

$$\mathbf{M}_{x,w,w',\Delta i,\Delta j} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{Q \times Q} \text{ for } x, w, w' \in \{0,1\}, \Delta i, \Delta j \in \{0,\pm 1\}:$$

$$\mathbf{M}_{x,w,w',\Delta i,\Delta j}[q,q'] = \begin{cases} 1, & \text{if } \left((q,x,w), (q',w',\Delta i,\Delta j)\right) \in \delta; \\ 0, & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

The transition matrix of M for input of length N and space bound S is MN,S ∈ {0, 1} <sup>C</sup>M,N,S×CM,N,S :

$$\mathbf{M}_{N,S}(\mathbf{x})[(i,j,\mathbf{W},\llcorner),(i',j',\mathbf{W}',\llcorner)] = \begin{cases} \mathbf{M}_{\mathbf{x}[i],\mathbf{W}[j],\mathbf{W}'[j],i'-i,j'-j}, & \text{if } i'-i,j'-j \in \{0,\pm 1\} \text{ and} \\ & \mathbf{W}[j''] = \mathbf{W}'[j''] \text{ for all } j'' \neq j; \\ \mathbf{0}, & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

It is readily verified that in MN,S(x)[(i, j,W, ),( , , , )], each transition block appears at most once. The matrix multiplication formula finds the number of accepting paths:

<span id="page-74-1"></span>Lemma 40. Let M = (Q, yacc, δ) be an NTM, x ∈ {0, 1} <sup>N</sup> for some N ≥ 1 and T, S ≥ 1. The number of valid computation paths reaching internal configuration (i, j,W, q) when running M starting from the initial configuration (1, 1, 0S, 1) with input x ∈ {0, 1} <sup>N</sup> and space bound S for T steps is

<span id="page-74-0"></span>
$$\left(\mathbf{e}_{(1,1,\mathbf{0}_S,1)}^{\mathsf{T}}\big(\mathbf{M}_{N,S}(\mathbf{x})\big)^T\right)^{\mathsf{T}}[(i,j,\mathbf{W},q)].$$

Moreover, computing e T (1,1,0S,1)MN,S(x) T 1[N]×[S]×{0,1} <sup>S</sup> ⊗yacc over the integers yields the number of accepting computation paths when running M starting from the initial configuration (1, 1, 0S, 1) with input x ∈ {0, 1} <sup>N</sup> within time T and space S, which is non-zero if and only if M accepts x within time T and space S.

Given the above lemma, we arithmetize time/space bounded computation of M by defining

$$M|_{N,T,S}(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{e}_{(1,1,\mathbf{0}_S,1)}^{\mathsf{T}} (\mathbf{M}_{N,S}(\mathbf{x}))^{T} (\mathbf{1}_{[N]\times[S]\times\{0,1\}^S} \otimes \mathbf{y}_{\mathrm{acc}}) \quad \text{over } \mathbb{Z}_p \text{ for } \mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^N, \tag{10}$$

which evaluates to 0 if (but not necessarily only if) x ∈ {0, 1} <sup>N</sup> is not accepted by M within time T and space S.

Discussion on Correctness. The arithmetization is precise for the complexity class modZpL [\[BDHM92\]](#page-80-10) (restricted logspace counting class mod p). Each language L ∈ modZpL is associated with a nondeterministic logspace Turing machine M such that

$$\mathbf{x} \in L \implies \#[\text{accepting paths of } M(\mathbf{x})] \not\equiv 0 \pmod{p},$$
  
 $\mathbf{x} \notin L \implies \#[\text{accepting paths of } M(\mathbf{x})] = 0 \text{ (as an integer)}.$ 

As a special case, UL ⊆ modZpL for all p, where UL means unambiguous logspace — these are languages recognized by a logspace NTM such that for any input, there is at most 1 accepting path.

However, it is not known whether NL ⊆ modZpL for any p. Indeed, if the number of accepting paths is a non-zero multiple of p, the computation will be mistakenly rejected under our arithmetization. We circumvent this correctness issue by sampling an instance of pairing groups with entropic order.

**Definition 41.** A family of pairing groups is  $\mathcal{G}$  with an efficient algorithm  $\mathcal{G}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda)$  (called pairing group sampler) that samples an instance  $(p, G_1, G_2, G_T, g_1, g_2, e)$  from some distribution of pairing groups such that

- $G_1, G_2, G_T$  are groups of the same prime order p;
- $G_1, G_2$  are generated by  $g_1, g_2$ , respectively;
- $e: G_1 \times G_2 \to G_T$  is bilinear and non-degenerate; and
- the group operations and e are efficiently computable, given the instance description.

The family has entropic order if the min-entropy of p is  $\omega(\log \lambda)$ :

$$H_{\lambda} = -\log_2 \max_{p^{\star}} \Pr\Big[(p, G_1, G_2, G_{\mathrm{T}}, g_1, g_2, e) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \, : \, p = p^{\star}\Big].$$

Bracket notations are meaningful once an instance is fixed. The definitions of MDDH and DDH (Definitions 1 and 3) are changed accordingly so that the groups are sampled by  $\mathcal{G}$ . Setup and the description is given to the distinguisher along with the challenge. Moreover, the definition of IPFE (Definition 6) can easily generalize to work with families of pairing groups (still with perfect correctness). The construction (see Appendix A) implying Lemma 8 also works with families of pairing groups and retains perfect correctness.

For 1-ABE and ABE, we relax the correctness requirement so that decapsulation or decryption only needs to succeed with overwhelming probability for polynomial-sized inputs.

**Definition 42** (1-ABE, altered). Let  $\mathcal{G}$  be a pairing group sampler. A 1-ABE scheme based on  $\mathcal{G}$  has the same syntax as in Definition 23, except that

- Setup additionally takes an instance of pairing groups as input.
- The correctness requirement is that for all sequence  $\{z_{\lambda} = (P_{\lambda} \in \mathcal{P}_{\lambda}, y_{\lambda} \in Y_{P_{\lambda}}, x_{\lambda} \in X_{P_{\lambda}})\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  such that the description of  $z_{\lambda}$  is polynomially long in  $\lambda$  and  $P_{\lambda}(x_{\lambda}, y_{\lambda}) = 1$  for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , the following probability is negligible in  $\lambda$ :

$$\Pr \begin{bmatrix} \mathsf{groups} = (p,\,G_1,G_2,G_{\mathrm{T}},\,g_1,g_2,\,e) \\ & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{G}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \\ \mathsf{msk} & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{groups},P_{\lambda}) \\ \mu & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p \\ \mathsf{sk} & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{msk},y,\mu) \\ \mathsf{ct} & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Enc}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{msk},x) \end{bmatrix}.$$

In the security experiments, the adversary additionally receives the instance description used to set up the 1-ABE scheme before making any queries.

It is also straightforward to relax the correctness requirement of ABE likewise. We now proceed to AKGS, 1-ABE and ABE schemes for NL.

**AKGS, 1-ABE and ABE for NL.** We simply plug in the matrix multiplication formula for NTM into AKGS, 1-ABE and ABE constructions.

<span id="page-76-0"></span>Construction 43 (AKGS, 1-ABE and ABE for NL). Let  $\mathcal{G}$  be a family of pairing groups. The AKGS, 1-ABE and ABE for NL is obtained by plugging Equation (10) into Constructions 30 (AKGS), 33 (1-ABE) and 37 (ABE) with (secret-key or slotted) IPFE based on  $\mathcal{G}$ . Formally, 1-ABE and ABE work for the following singleton predicate space:

$$X = \left\{ (\mathbf{x}, 1^T, 1^{2^S}) \, \middle| \, \mathbf{x} \in \{0, 1\}^N \text{ for some } N \ge 1, \, T, S \ge 1 \right\}, \quad Y = \left\{ M \, \middle| \, M \text{ is an NTM} \right\},$$

$$P : X \times Y \to \{0, 1\}, \, \left( (\mathbf{x}, 1^T, 1^{2^S}), M \right) \mapsto \begin{cases} 1, & \text{if } M \text{ accepts } \mathbf{x} \text{ within} \\ & \text{time } T \text{ and space } S; \\ 0, & \text{otherwise;} \end{cases}$$

$$\mathcal{P}_{\lambda} = \{P\}, \quad \mathcal{P} = \{\mathcal{P}_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}.$$

In 1-ABE and ABE, Dec returns  $\perp$  if  $M|_{N,T,S}(\mathbf{x}) \equiv 0 \pmod{p}$ ; otherwise, it recovers  $\mu$  or  $\overline{\mu}$  by dividing the Eval result by  $M|_{N,T,S}(\mathbf{x})$ . In ABE, the message space is chosen so that it is efficiently encodable and decodable in the target group given the instance description.

**Theorem 44.** The AKGS in Construction 43 is linear, correct, and special piecewise secure with the same ordering of label functions and randomizers as described in Theorem 31.

Suppose in Construction 43,  $\mathcal{G}$  has entropic order, SXDH holds in  $\mathcal{G}$  and the IPFE schemes are function-hiding, then the constructed 1-ABE and ABE are correct (in the relaxed sense) and secure.

<u>Proof.</u> The AKGS linearity, correctness and special piecewise security (and ordering) are straightforward. The security of 1-ABE and ABE also generalizes from the proof of Theorems 34 and 38 — by Lemma 40,  $M|_{N,T,S}$  for non-deterministic Turing machine M always evaluates to 0 if the input is not accepted within the time/space bounds. We focus on the (relaxed) correctness of 1-ABE and ABE, which amounts to showing that  $M|_{N,T,S}(\mathbf{x}) \equiv 0 \pmod{p}$  with only negligible probability for accepting computations.

Let the sequence in the correctness test be 17

$$z_{\lambda} = (P, M_{\lambda}, \mathbf{x}_{\lambda}, 1^{T_{\lambda}}, 1^{2^{S_{\lambda}}}), \quad P \text{ is the only predicate,} \quad M_{\lambda} = (Q_{\lambda}, \mathbf{y}_{\text{acc}, \lambda}, \delta_{\lambda}), \quad \mathbf{x}_{\lambda} \in \{0, 1\}^{N_{\lambda}}.$$

Since  $z_{\lambda}$  can only be polynomially long, the number of possible transitions (i.e.,  $|\delta_{\lambda}|$ ) and the time constraint  $T_{\lambda}$  are bounded by polynomial of  $\lambda$ , which implies that

$$A_{\lambda} \stackrel{\text{def}}{=\!\!\!=\!\!\!=} \#[\text{accepting paths of } M(\mathbf{x}) \text{ in } T \text{ steps}]$$

$$\leq \#[\text{valid computation paths of } M(\mathbf{x}) \text{ in } T \text{ steps}]$$

$$\leq |\delta_{\lambda}|^{T_{\lambda}} \leq 2^{c+\lambda^{c}}$$

for some constant c>0. In particular, this means  $A_{\lambda}$  has no more than  $c+\lambda^c$  prime factors. Let the min-entropy of group order p be  $H_{\lambda}=\omega(\log\lambda)$ . By our assumption,  $M_{\lambda}$  accepts  $\mathbf{x}_{\lambda}$  within time  $T_{\lambda}$  and space  $S_{\lambda}$ , so  $A_{\lambda}>0$  and

$$\Pr\left[(p,\ldots) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{G}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \, : \, p \mid A_{\lambda}\right] \leq \hspace{-0.2em} \sum_{\substack{p' \text{ prime} \\ p' \mid A_{\lambda}}} \hspace{-0.2em} \Pr\left[(p,\ldots) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{G}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \, : \, p = p'\right] \leq (c + \lambda^{c}) 2^{-H_{\lambda}}$$
 is negligible in  $\lambda$ .

<u>Remarks</u>. If the non-deterministic machine in question is *unambiguous*, the schemes can be instantiated with a sequence of pairing groups with perfect correctness.

<span id="page-76-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>17</sup>In case of ABE, there is also a message, but it does not affect the argument.

### <span id="page-77-0"></span>7.5 ABE for DFA/NFA

A deterministic finite automaton (DFA) can be converted (in polynomial time) to a deterministic Turing machine with space complexity 1 and time complexity N, where N is the length of the input. Similarly, an NFA can be converted to an NTM with space complexity 1 and time complexity N. Therefore, ABE schemes for DFA and NFA are just special cases of our ABE for L and NL.

As an optimization, DFA and NFA do not need to keep track of the input tape pointer in their internal configurations — it is always the current time step. This corresponds to a simpler formula (thus a simpler AKGS) for arithmetizing DFA and NFA. We formally define NFA (with DFA as a special kind of NFA), present the AKGS for it, and discuss how to construct 1-ABE and ABE for NFA. Again, we only consider the binary alphabet. The schemes readily extend to handle any polynomial-sized alphabet fixed at set-up time.

Definition 45. A non-deterministic finite automaton is a tuple (Q, yacc, δ), where Q ≥ 1 is the number of states (we use [Q] as the set of states and 1 the initial state), yacc ∈ {0, 1} Q indicates whether each state is accepting, and δ is a relation (state transition relation) between [Q] × {0, 1} and [Q]. For x ∈ {0, 1} <sup>N</sup> for some N ≥ 1, the NFA accepts x if there exists q0, . . . , q<sup>N</sup> ∈ [Q] (called an accepting path) such that

$$q_0 = 1, \quad ((q_{i-1}, \mathbf{x}[i]), q_i) \in \delta, \quad \mathbf{y}_{acc}[q_N] = 1.$$

A path without the last condition is a computation path. An NFA is deterministic, if δ is a function relation. An NFA is unambiguous, if for any input x, there is at most 1 accepting path.

Clearly, a DFA is always unambiguous.

Transition Matrix and Blocks. We use e<sup>q</sup> ∈ {0, 1} Q to represent the current state of an NFA. For an NFA M = (Q, yacc, δ), its transition matrix is

$$\mathbf{M}(x)[q, q'] = \begin{cases} 1, & \text{if } ((q, x), q') \in \delta; \\ 0, & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

For all q ∈ [Q] and x ∈ {0, 1}, consider c <sup>T</sup> = e T <sup>q</sup>M(x) — we have c ∈ {0, 1} Q and c[q 0 ] = 1 if and only if q 0 is a valid state after the NFA reads x in state q. Inductively, e T <sup>q</sup>M(x1)· · ·M(xn) is a vector that counts the number of computation paths reaching each state starting from state q after reading x1, . . . , xn. Let the transition blocks be M<sup>x</sup> = M(x) for x ∈ {0, 1}, then M(x) = (1 − x)M<sup>0</sup> +xM1. We arithmetize the computation of NFA by defining

$$M|_{N}(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{e}_{1}^{\mathsf{T}} \prod_{i=1}^{N} \left( (1 - \mathbf{x}[i]) \mathbf{M}_{0} + \mathbf{x}[i] \mathbf{M}_{1} \right) \cdot \mathbf{y}_{\mathrm{acc}} \quad \text{over } \mathbb{Z}_{p} \text{ for } \mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{N}.$$
 (11)

In case the NFA is unambiguous, M|<sup>N</sup> is binary over {0, 1} <sup>N</sup> and indicates whether M accepts the input.

AKGS for NFA. Garbling M|<sup>N</sup> using the recursive mechanism for garbling matrix multiplication yields a special piecewise secure AKGS for NFA.

<span id="page-77-1"></span>Construction 46. Let F = {(M, 1 <sup>N</sup> , p)| M is an NFA, p prime} be the function class, i.e., M|<sup>N</sup> over Z<sup>p</sup> is encoded as (M, 1 <sup>N</sup> , p). The AKGS (Garble, Eval) for F operates as follows:

• Garble (M, 1 <sup>N</sup> , p), α, β takes the NFA and the secrets as input. It computes the transition blocks M0,M<sup>1</sup> for M, samples r0, . . . , r<sup>N</sup> \$← Z Q <sup>p</sup> , and defines the label functions by

$$L_{\text{init}}(\mathbf{x}) = \beta + \mathbf{e}_{1}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{r}_{0},$$
for  $i \in [N]$ :  $(L_{i,q}(\mathbf{x}))_{q \in [Q]} = -\mathbf{r}_{i-1} + ((1 - \mathbf{x}[i])\mathbf{M}_{0} + \mathbf{x}[i]\mathbf{M}_{1})\mathbf{r}_{i},$ 

$$(L_{N+1,q}(\mathbf{x}))_{q \in [Q]} = -\mathbf{r}_{N} + \alpha \mathbf{y}_{\text{acc}}.$$

The algorithm collects the coefficient vectors and outputs them.

• Eval (M, 1 <sup>N</sup> , p), x, `init,(`i,q)i∈[N+1],q∈[Q] takes the NFA, the input string x ∈ Z N <sup>p</sup> and the labels as input. It computes the transition blocks M0,M<sup>1</sup> of M, sets `<sup>i</sup> = (`i,q)q∈[Q] for i ∈ [N + 1], and computes and returns

$$\ell_{\text{init}} + \mathbf{e}_1^{\mathsf{T}} \sum_{i=1}^{N+1} \prod_{j=1}^{i-1} \left( (1 - \mathbf{x}[j]) \mathbf{M}_0 + \mathbf{x}[j] \mathbf{M}_1 \right) \cdot \boldsymbol{\ell}_i.$$

Note: It is straightforward to verify the construction satisfies the syntax of a linear AKGS. The evaluation correctness can be readily verified (similar to Construction [30\)](#page-47-2).

Remarks. Construction [46](#page-77-1) coincides with the secret-sharing scheme for DFA by Waters [\[Wat12\]](#page-84-2). However, extending it to NFA via path-tracking makes the secret-sharing scheme insecure due to backtracking attacks, as observed in [\[Wat12\]](#page-84-2). In contrast, generalizing it to NFA via path-counting using the matrix multiplication formula retains the special piecewise security.

Theorem 47. Construction [46](#page-77-1) is special piecewise secure with Linit being the first label function, the other label functions sorted in increasing order of i, and the randomness sorted in the same order as the label functions.

ABE for NFA. ABE for NFA can be obtained following the same blueprint of Constructions [33](#page-50-2) and [37](#page-65-0) and using a family of pairing groups with entropic order. With the pseudorandomness set to r<sup>i</sup> = rx[i]r<sup>f</sup> for r<sup>x</sup> \$← Z [0..N] <sup>p</sup> sampled at encryption time and r<sup>f</sup> \$← Z Q <sup>p</sup> sampled at key generation time, the labels for α = µ, β = 0 can be computed as

$$\begin{split} \ell_{\text{init}} &= \mathbf{r}_0[1] = \mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{x}}[0]\mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{f}}[1] = \langle \mathbf{u}_{\text{init}}, \mathbf{v}_{\text{init}} \rangle, \\ \text{for } i \in [N], q \in [Q] \colon & \ell_{i,q} = -\mathbf{r}_{i-1}[q] + \left( ((1 - \mathbf{x}[i])\mathbf{M}_0 + \mathbf{x}[i]\mathbf{M}_1)\mathbf{r}_i \right)[q] \\ &= -\mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{x}}[i-1]\mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{f}}[q] + \mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{x}}[i](1 - \mathbf{x}[i])(\mathbf{M}_0\mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{f}})[q] + \mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{x}}[i]\mathbf{x}[i](\mathbf{M}_1\mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{f}})[q] \\ &= \langle \mathbf{u}_i, \mathbf{v}_q \rangle, \\ \text{for } q \in [Q] \colon & \ell_{N+1,q} = -\mathbf{r}_N[q] + \mu\mathbf{y}_{\text{acc}}[q] = -\mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{x}}[N]\mathbf{r}_{\mathbf{f}}[q] + \mu\mathbf{y}_{\text{acc}}[q] = \langle \mathbf{u}_{N+1}, \mathbf{v}_q \rangle, \end{split}$$

where the vectors are as follows:

| vector      | init      | rand            | acc      | tb0                   | tb1       | the other<br>indices |
|-------------|-----------|-----------------|----------|-----------------------|-----------|----------------------|
| uinit       | rx[0]     | 0               | 0        | 0                     | 0         |                      |
| vinit       | rf<br>[1] | 0               | 0        | 0                     | 0         |                      |
| i ∈ [N]: ui | 0         | rx[i<br>−<br>1] | 0        | rx[i](1<br>−<br>x[i]) | rx[i]x[i] | 0                    |
| uN+1        | 0         | rx[N]           | 1        | 0                     | 0         |                      |
| q ∈ [Q]: vq | 0         | −rf<br>[q]      | µyacc[q] | (M0rf)[q]             | (M1rf)[q] |                      |

As is the case for the other 1-ABE schemes, 1-ABE for NFA will need extra indices for the security proof, the exact number of which can be figured out easily but tediously. Likewise, the ABE scheme will need more copies of the indices for computing the labels (cf. Construction [37\)](#page-65-0).

Corollary 48. Assuming a family of pairing groups with entropic order in which SXDH holds, there is a compact and adaptively secure ABE for NFA (satisfying the relaxed correctness requirement). If the NFA are restricted to unambiguous ones, the scheme can be instantiated in a sequence of pairing groups with perfect correctness. Moreover, SXDH can be relaxed to MDDH<sup>k</sup> in both of the source groups for any integer k ≥ 1.

## <span id="page-85-0"></span>A Construction of Function-Hiding Slotted IPFE

In this section, we construct the function-hiding slotted IPFE promised in Lemma 8 by applying the double encryption technique [LV16,Lin17] to the adaptively secure public-key encryption scheme in [ALS16,Wee17].

First, we recall the adaptively secure public-key IPFE scheme in [ALS16,Wee17] in our syntax. Note that it suffices to consider index sets being  $\mathfrak{s} = [n]$ .

<span id="page-85-1"></span>Construction 49 ([ALS16,Wee17]). We describe the construction for any fixed value of the security parameter  $\lambda$  and suppress the appearance of  $\lambda$  below for simplicity of notations. Let  $\mathcal{G}$  be pairing groups of order p such that the MDDH<sub>k</sub> assumption holds in  $G_1$ . The public-key IPFE scheme (Setup, KeyGen, Enc, Dec) based on  $\mathcal{G}$  operates as follows:

• Setup( $1^n$ ) takes the dimension of vectors in unary (i.e., [n] is encoded as  $1^n$ ) as input and generates the master public/secret key pair by

$$\begin{split} \mathbf{A} & \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times (k+1)}, & \mathbf{W} & \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(k+1) \times n}, \\ \mathsf{mpk} &= ([\![\mathbf{A}]\!]_1, [\![\mathbf{AW}]\!]_1), & \mathsf{msk} &= \mathbf{W}. \end{split}$$

- KeyGen(msk,  $[\![\mathbf{v}]\!]_2$ ) takes the master secret key msk =  $\mathbf{W}$  and a vector  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  (encoded in  $G_2$ ) as input. It returns sk =  $(-\mathbf{W}[\![\mathbf{v}]\!]_2, [\![\mathbf{v}]\!]_2)$  as the secret key for  $\mathbf{v}$ .
- $\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{mpk}, \llbracket \mathbf{u} \rrbracket_1)$  takes the master public key  $\mathsf{mpk} = (\llbracket \mathbf{A} \rrbracket_1, \llbracket \mathbf{AW} \rrbracket_1)$  and a vector  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  (encoded in  $G_1$ ) as input. It generates the ciphertext for  $\mathbf{u}$  by

$$\mathbf{s} \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k, \quad \mathsf{ct} = (\mathbf{s}^{\scriptscriptstyle\mathsf{T}} [\![ \mathbf{A} ]\!]_1, \mathbf{s}^{\scriptscriptstyle\mathsf{T}} [\![ \mathbf{A} \mathbf{W} ]\!]_1 + [\![ \mathbf{u}^{\scriptscriptstyle\mathsf{T}} ]\!]_1).$$

• Dec(sk, ct) takes a secret key and a ciphertext as input. It parses sk as  $([\![\mathbf{y}]\!]_2, [\![\mathbf{w}]\!]_2)$  and ct as  $([\![\mathbf{z}^T]\!]_1, [\![\mathbf{x}^T]\!]_1)$  and returns  $[\![\mathbf{z}^T]\!]_1 [\![\mathbf{w}]\!]_2 + [\![\mathbf{x}^T]\!]_1 [\![\mathbf{w}]\!]_2$  as the decryption result.

Note: The correctness of the scheme is readily verified by the equation

$$\mathbf{z}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{y} + \mathbf{x}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{w} = (\mathbf{s}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{A})(-\mathbf{W}\mathbf{v}) + (\mathbf{s}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{A}\mathbf{W} + \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T})\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{v}.$$

The (standard) IND-CPA security of a public-key IPFE scheme can be defined using the experiments for function-hiding security of slotted IPFE (Definition 7) by setting  $\mathfrak{s}_{priv} = \emptyset$  and  $\mathfrak{s}_{pub} = \mathfrak{s}$ — this means  $\mathbf{v}_j^0 = \mathbf{v}_j^1$  in the challenges, i.e., only the ciphertext vector may vary. The above construction satisfies this notion.

Lemma 50 ([ALS16,Wee17]). Construction 49 is IND-CPA secure.

<u>Remarks.</u> In [ALS16,Wee17], Construction 49 is presented in a (not necessarily pairing) group in which MDDH<sub>k</sub> holds. In particular, the secret key vector and the secret key are vectors encoded in  $\mathbb{Z}_p$  instead of  $G_2$ . We encode them in  $G_2$  only to adhere to the formalism used in this work. Moreover, for any group  $G_1$  of order p, we can build a "pairing group" by setting  $G_2 = \mathbb{Z}_p$  (additive group of integers modulo p) and  $e(g,h) = g^h$ . The additional benefit of using this formalism, as we shall see soon, is that certain preconditions of applying the technique in [LV16,Lin17] can be simplified.

We note that Construction 49 has four special properties:

- A secret key is a vector (of dimension (k+1)+n) encoded in  $G_2$ .
- A ciphertext is a vector (of dimension (k+1)+n) encoded in  $G_1$ .
- The secret key for  $\mathbf{0}$  is  $\mathbf{0}$  (encoded in  $G_2$ ) (or rather, any distribution known with only mpk).
- The decryption algorithm simply computes the inner product of the secret key and the ciphertext using the pairing operation.

The first two properties suggest that it is syntactically possible to nest IPFE schemes. The last property guarantees that nesting preserves correctness. The key idea of the double encryption technique in [LV16,Lin17] is to nest IPFE schemes, one using  $G_1$  for ciphertexts and the other using  $G_1$  for secret keys, so that both secret keys and ciphertexts in the resulting scheme are protected by one instance of public-key IPFE.

**Definition 51.** Let  $\mathcal{G} = (G_1, G_2, G_T, g_1, g_2, e)$  be pairing groups. Its opposite is

$$\mathcal{G}^{\text{op}} = (G_2, G_1, G_T, g_2, g_1, e^{\text{op}}), \quad e^{\text{op}}(h_2, h_1) = e(h_1, h_2).$$

Clearly, given a sequence of pairing groups, taking the opposite yields another sequence of pairing groups whose operations are still efficient (using the algorithms for the original sequence). Armed with this notion, we are ready to apply the double encryption technique tailored to our needs.

<span id="page-86-0"></span>Construction 52 (slotted IPFE [LV16,Lin17]). We describe the construction for any fixed value of the security parameter  $\lambda$  and suppress the appearance of  $\lambda$  below for simplicity of notations. Let  $\mathcal{G}$  be pairing groups of order p such that the MDDH<sub>k</sub> assumption holds in both  $G_1$  and  $G_2$ , and let  $\mathcal{G}^{op}$  be its opposite. Instantiate Construction 49 to obtain

(outer scheme) (Setup<sub>out</sub>, KeyGen<sub>out</sub>, Enc<sub>out</sub>, Dec<sub>out</sub>) based on 
$$\mathcal{G}$$
 and (inner scheme) (Setup<sub>in</sub>, KeyGen<sub>in</sub>, Enc<sub>in</sub>, Dec<sub>in</sub>) based on  $\mathcal{G}^{op}$ .

The slotted IPFE scheme based on  $\mathcal{G}$  operates as follows:

• Setup( $1^{n_{\text{pub}}}, 1^{n_{\text{priv}}}$ ) takes the public/private slot lengths in unary as input (i.e.,  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}} = [n_{\text{pub}}]$  and  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}} = [n_{\text{pub}} + n_{\text{priv}}] \setminus [n_{\text{pub}}]$ ). It generates master public/secret key pairs for both inner and outer public-key IPFE schemes:

$$(\mathsf{mpk}_\mathsf{in},\mathsf{msk}_\mathsf{in}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}_\mathsf{in}(1^{2n_{\mathrm{priv}}}), \quad (\mathsf{mpk}_\mathsf{out},\mathsf{msk}_\mathsf{out}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}_\mathsf{out}(1^{n_{\mathrm{pub}}+(k+1)+2n_{\mathrm{priv}}}).$$

The algorithm returns  $mpk = mpk_{out}$  and  $msk = (mpk_{in}, msk_{in}, mpk_{out}, msk_{out})$ .

Note: We explain the choice of parameters. The inner scheme is used to encrypt the private slot, and only the first  $n_{\text{priv}}$  components are used in the honest algorithms — the extra  $n_{\text{priv}}$  components provide programming space in the security proof. The outer scheme is used to encrypt the whole vector. The first  $n_{\text{pub}}$  components store the public slot, and the other  $(k+1)+2n_{\text{priv}}$  components store the secret key/ciphertext of the private slot.

For notational simplicity, we will suppress the master secret (resp. public) key as an input to key generation (resp. encryption) algorithms of the inner/outer schemes — there is only one natural choice for each of them.

• KeyGen(msk,  $[\![\mathbf{v}]\!]_2$ ) takes the master secret key msk and a vector  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n_{\text{pub}}+n_{\text{priv}}}$  (encoded in  $G_2$ ) as input. It separates  $\mathbf{v}$  by slots, i.e.,  $\mathbf{v} = (\mathbf{v}_{\text{pub}}, \mathbf{v}_{\text{priv}})$  with  $\mathbf{v}_{\text{pub}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n_{\text{pub}}}$  and  $\mathbf{v}_{\text{priv}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n_{\text{priv}}}$ , and generates the secret key by

$$\mathsf{sk} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{KeyGen}_\mathsf{out}\big([\![\mathbf{v}_\mathrm{pub}]\!]_2, \mathsf{Enc}_\mathsf{in}([\![\mathbf{v}_\mathrm{priv}]\!]_2, [\![\mathbf{0}_{n_\mathrm{priv}}]\!]_2)\big).$$

• Enc(msk,  $[\![\mathbf{u}]\!]_1$ ) takes the master secret key msk and a vector  $\mathbf{u} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n_{\text{pub}}+n_{\text{priv}}}$  (encoded in  $G_1$ ) as input. It separates  $\mathbf{u}$  by slots into  $\mathbf{u}_{\text{pub}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n_{\text{pub}}}$  and  $\mathbf{u}_{\text{priv}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n_{\text{priv}}}$ , and generates the ciphertext by

$$\mathsf{ct} \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{out}}\big([\![\mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{pub}}]\!]_1, \mathsf{KeyGen}_{\mathsf{in}}([\![\mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{priv}}]\!]_1, [\![\mathbf{0}_{n_{\mathrm{priv}}}]\!]_1)\big).$$

• Dec(sk, ct) returns Dec<sub>out</sub>(sk, ct).

Note: We verify the correctness of the scheme. By the correctness of the outer scheme,

$$\mathsf{Dec}_\mathsf{out}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}) = [\![\langle \mathbf{u}_\mathrm{pub}, \mathbf{v}_\mathrm{pub} \rangle + \langle \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} \rangle]\!]_\mathrm{T},$$

where  $\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}$  are the vectors encoded in the secret key/ciphertext of the inner scheme. By the correctness of the inner scheme and the fact that decryption computes  $\langle \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} \rangle$  in the exponent in the target group, we have

$$\langle \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} \rangle = \langle (\mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{priv}}, \mathbf{0}_{n_{\mathrm{priv}}}), (\mathbf{v}_{\mathrm{priv}}, \mathbf{0}_{n_{\mathrm{priv}}}) \rangle = \langle \mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{priv}}, \mathbf{v}_{\mathrm{priv}} \rangle$$

 $\textit{Combining the two gives} \ \mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}) = [\![\langle \mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{pub}},\mathbf{v}_{\mathrm{pub}}\rangle + \langle \mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{priv}},\mathbf{v}_{\mathrm{priv}}\rangle]\!]_{\mathrm{T}} = [\![\langle \mathbf{u},\mathbf{v}\rangle]\!]_{\mathrm{T}}.$ 

• SlotEnc(mpk,  $[\mathbf{u}_{\text{pub}}]_1$ ) takes the master public key mpk and a vector  $\mathbf{u}_{\text{pub}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n_{\text{pub}}}$  (encoded in  $G_1$ ) as input. It generates the ciphertext by

$$\mathsf{ct} \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{out}}\big([\![\mathbf{u}_{\mathrm{pub}}]\!]_1,[\![\mathbf{0}_{(k+1)+2n_{\mathrm{priv}}}]\!]_1\big).$$

Note: SlotEnc and Enc generates identically distributed ciphertexts if  $\mathbf{u}_{\text{priv}} = \mathbf{0}_{n_{\text{priv}}}$  — this is because the secret key for the zero vector in Construction 49 is the zero vector. This construction can be instantiated as long as the distribution of the secret key for the zero vector is efficiently sampleable given  $\mathsf{mpk}_{\mathsf{in}}$  (and  $\mathsf{mpk}$  will include  $\mathsf{mpk}_{\mathsf{in}}$ ).

<span id="page-87-0"></span>**Theorem 53** (Lemma 8). Construction 52 is function-hiding (Definition 7).

<u>Proof.</u> We show  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{\mathrm{FH}} \approx \mathsf{Exp}^1_{\mathrm{FH}}$  by going through several hybrids responding to the challenges in different ways. For  $\mathbf{z}_1 \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n_{\mathrm{pub}}}$  and  $\mathbf{z}_2, \mathbf{z}_3 \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n_{\mathrm{priv}}}$ , we write

$$\begin{aligned} & \mathsf{sk} \sim \mathbf{z}_1 \ \mathbf{z}_2 \ \mathbf{z}_3 \quad \mathrm{for} \quad \mathsf{sk} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{KeyGen}_{\mathsf{out}}\big([\![\mathbf{z}_1]\!]_2, \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{in}}([\![\mathbf{z}_2]\!]_2, [\![\mathbf{z}_3]\!]_2)\big) \\ \mathrm{and} \quad \mathsf{ct} \sim \mathbf{z}_1 \ \mathbf{z}_2 \ \mathbf{z}_3 \quad \mathrm{for} \quad \mathsf{ct} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{out}}\big([\![\mathbf{z}_1]\!]_1, \mathsf{KeyGen}_{\mathsf{in}}\big([\![\mathbf{z}_2]\!]_1, [\![\mathbf{z}_3]\!]_1)\big). \end{aligned}$$

Consider the hybrids illustrated in Figure 12. Observe the vectors encoded by the inner scheme:

$$\begin{split} & \qquad \qquad & \text{in } \mathsf{sk}_{i,\mathsf{in}} \text{ (in } \mathsf{ct}_{i,\mathsf{out}}) & \qquad & \mathsf{in } \mathsf{ct}_{j,\mathsf{in}} \text{ (in } \mathsf{sk}_{j,\mathsf{out}}) \\ \mathsf{H}_0 \to \mathsf{H}_1 \colon \left\langle \left( \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{u}_{i,\mathsf{priv}}^0 &, & \underline{\mathbf{0}} \\ \end{array} \right), \left( \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{v}_{j,\mathsf{priv}}^0 &, & \mathbf{0} \to \mathbf{v}_{j,\mathsf{priv}}^1 \end{array} \right) \right\rangle, \\ \mathsf{H}_2 \to \mathsf{H}_3 \colon \left\langle \left( \begin{array}{c} \underline{\mathbf{0}} \\ \end{array} \right, & \mathbf{u}_{i,\mathsf{priv}}^1 \end{array} \right), \left( \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{v}_{j,\mathsf{priv}}^0 \to \mathbf{v}_{j,\mathsf{priv}}^1 &, & \mathbf{v}_{j,\mathsf{priv}}^1 \to \mathbf{0} \end{array} \right) \right\rangle, \\ \mathsf{H}_4 \to \mathsf{H}_5 \colon \left\langle \left( \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{u}_{i,\mathsf{priv}}^1 &, & \underline{\mathbf{0}} \\ \end{array} \right), \left( \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{v}_{j,\mathsf{priv}}^1 &, & \mathbf{v}_{j,\mathsf{priv}}^1 \to \mathbf{0} \end{array} \right) \right\rangle. \end{split}$$

For those three transitions, we only modify the ciphertext vectors of the inner scheme, and the modified part only multiplies with  $\mathbf{0}$  (underlined) in the secret keys (of the inner scheme). Therefore,  $H_0 \approx H_1, H_2 \approx H_3$  and  $H_4 \approx H_5$  reduce to the IND-CPA security of the inner scheme. (The reduction will run  $\mathsf{Setup}_\mathsf{out}$  on its own.)

<span id="page-88-0"></span>

| hybrid          | respond to the challenges by                               |  |  |  |
|-----------------|------------------------------------------------------------|--|--|--|
| Exp0<br>FH ≡ H0 | 0<br>skj<br>∼<br>vj,pub<br>v<br>0<br>j,priv                |  |  |  |
|                 | 0<br>0<br>cti<br>∼<br>u<br>u<br>0<br>i,pub<br>i,priv       |  |  |  |
|                 | 0<br>1<br>skj<br>∼<br>vj,pub<br>v<br>v<br>j,priv<br>j,priv |  |  |  |
| H1              | 0<br>0<br>cti<br>∼<br>u<br>u<br>0<br>i,pub<br>i,priv       |  |  |  |
|                 | 0<br>1<br>∼<br>skj<br>vj,pub<br>v<br>v<br>j,priv<br>j,priv |  |  |  |
| H2              | 1<br>1<br>cti<br>∼<br>u<br>0<br>u<br>i,pub<br>i,priv       |  |  |  |
| H3              | 1<br>1<br>skj<br>∼<br>vj,pub<br>v<br>v<br>j,priv<br>j,priv |  |  |  |
|                 | 1<br>1<br>∼<br>cti<br>u<br>0<br>u<br>i,priv<br>i,pub       |  |  |  |
|                 | 1<br>1<br>skj<br>∼<br>vj,pub<br>v<br>v<br>j,priv<br>j,priv |  |  |  |
| H4              | 1<br>1<br>cti<br>∼<br>u<br>u<br>0<br>i,pub<br>i,priv       |  |  |  |
| Exp1            | 1<br>skj<br>∼<br>vj,pub<br>v<br>0<br>j,priv                |  |  |  |
| FH ≡ H5         | 1<br>1<br>cti<br>∼<br>u<br>u<br>0<br>i,pub<br>i,priv       |  |  |  |

Figure 12: Hybrids for proving the function-hiding property of Construction [52.](#page-86-0)

The other two transitions follow by the IND-CPA security of the outer scheme, taking advantage of the fact that the decryption of the inner scheme is computing the inner product of the secret key and the ciphertext — this means the outer decryption implicitly decrypts the inner instance. In  $H_1$ , the inner products (for the outer scheme) are

$$\begin{split} \left\langle \left(\mathbf{u}_{i,\mathrm{pub}}^{0},\mathsf{KeyGen}_{\mathsf{in}}(\mathbf{u}_{i,\mathrm{priv}}^{0},\mathbf{0})\right),\left(\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{pub}},\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{in}}(\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{priv}}^{0},\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{priv}}^{1})\right)\right\rangle \\ &=\left\langle \mathbf{u}_{i,\mathrm{pub}}^{0},\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{pub}}\right\rangle +\left\langle \mathsf{KeyGen}_{\mathsf{in}}(\mathbf{u}_{i,\mathrm{pub}}^{0},\mathbf{0}),\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{in}}(\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{priv}}^{0},\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{priv}}^{1})\right\rangle \\ \left(\mathsf{Dec}_{\mathsf{in}}\;\mathsf{is}\;\mathsf{inner}\;\mathsf{product}\right) &=\left\langle \mathbf{u}_{i,\mathrm{pub}}^{0},\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{pub}}\right\rangle +\left\langle \left(\mathbf{u}_{i,\mathrm{pub}}^{0},\mathbf{0}\right),\left(\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{priv}}^{0},\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{priv}}^{1}\right)\right\rangle \\ &=\left\langle \mathbf{u}_{i,\mathrm{pub}}^{0},\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{pub}}\right\rangle +\left\langle \mathbf{u}_{i,\mathrm{priv}}^{0},\mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{priv}}^{0}\right\rangle. \end{split}$$

In  $H_2$ , the inner products are  $\langle \mathbf{u}^1_{i,\mathrm{pub}}, \mathbf{v}_{j,\mathrm{pub}} \rangle + \langle \mathbf{u}^1_{i,\mathrm{priv}}, \mathbf{v}^1_{j,\mathrm{priv}} \rangle$ , which remain unchanged by the constraint. Moreover, as far as the outer scheme is concerned, only the ciphertext vectors are changed, so  $H_1 \approx H_2$  reduces to the IND-CPA security of the outer scheme. Same for  $H_3$  and  $H_4$ . (The reduction will run  $\mathsf{Setup}_{\mathsf{in}}$  on its own.)

By a hybrid argument, we conclude  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{\mathrm{FH}} \equiv \mathsf{H}_0 \approx \mathsf{H}_5 \equiv \mathsf{Exp}^1_{\mathrm{FH}}.$ 

# <span id="page-89-0"></span>B Key Delegation

Key delegation in the context of ABE is the ability to securely create a more restrictive key from a secret key without the master secret key. This ability is characterized by a syntactical change of KeyGen and a generalized correctness requirement with respect to arbitrarily delegated keys. The experiments in the basic security notion (IND-CPA) are also adapted to take key delegation into account. In this section, we define the notion of key delegation and IND-CPA-DLG security for ABE, and show to how to tweak our basic KP-ABE scheme for ABPs to achieve these notions.

**Definition 54** (key delegation). In an ABE for message space  $\mathcal{M}$  and predicate space  $\mathcal{P}$  that supports delegation, the key generation algorithm KeyGen takes a generalized syntax and the correctness requirement is strengthened:

- KeyGen( $1^{\lambda}$ , mpk, sk $_{D:y_1,...,y_D}$ ,  $y_{D+1}$ ) takes the master **public** key, a secret key sk $_{D:y_1,...,y_D}$ , and a new policy  $y_{D+1} \in Y_P$  as input, where P is the predicate in use. Here, D represents the number of times the key is delegated and  $y_1, \ldots, y_D$  the previously imposed policies. The master secret key msk is alternatively denoted by sk $_0$ , i.e., a key delegated for 0 times. The algorithm outputs a delegated key sk $_{D+1:y_1,...,y_{D+1}}$ .
- Correctness holds with respect to arbitrarily delegated keys. Formally, for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all  $P \in \mathcal{P}_{\lambda}$ , all  $x \in X_P$ ,  $g \in M_{\lambda}$ , and all  $D \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $y_1, \ldots, y_D \in Y_P$  such that  $P(x, y_1) = \cdots = P(x, y_D) = 1$ , it holds that

$$\Pr \begin{bmatrix} (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk} \overset{\mathrm{def}}{=\!\!\!=} \mathsf{sk}_0) & \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{^\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, P) \\ d \in [D] \colon & \mathsf{sk}_d & \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{^\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^\lambda, \mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{sk}_{d-1}, y_d) \, \colon \mathsf{Dec}(1^\lambda, \mathsf{sk}_D, \mathsf{ct}) = g \\ & \mathsf{ct} & \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{^\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Enc}(1^\lambda, \mathsf{mpk}, x, g) \end{bmatrix} = 1.$$

In the security game taking delegation into account [SW08], the adversary, instead of making key queries, makes key delegation and key revelation queries. The former creates a delegated key, and the latter reveals a delegated key to the adversary. Security should hold as long as the challenge attribute is not permitted by any individual secret key that is *revealed* to the adversary. Note that in IND-CPA-DLG, merely creating a secret key does not impose restrictions on the challenge attribute.

**Definition 55** (IND-CPA-DLG [SW08]). Let (Setup, KeyGen, Enc, Dec) be an ABE for message space  $\mathcal{M}$  and predicate space  $\mathcal{P}$  supporting delegation. The scheme is IND-CPA-DLG secure if  $\mathsf{Exp}^0_{\mathsf{CPA-DLG}} \approx \mathsf{Exp}^1_{\mathsf{CPA-DLG}}$ , where  $\mathsf{Exp}^b_{\mathsf{CPA-DLG}}$  is defined as follows:

• Setup. Run  $\mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda})$  and receive a predicate  $P \in \mathcal{P}_{\lambda}$  from it. Run (mpk, msk)  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup $(1^{\lambda}, P)$ , define  $\mathsf{sk}_0 = \mathsf{msk}, Z_0 = ()$ , and let  $H \leftarrow \{0\}, R \leftarrow \emptyset$ . Return  $\mathsf{mpk}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

Note: Elements in H are key handles, each of which corresponds to a (delegated) secret key. The set R keeps track of the handles whose corresponding keys are revealed. For each handle  $h \in H$ , the list  $Z_h \in Y_P^*$  consists of the policies imposed on  $\mathsf{sk}_h$ .

- Query I. Repeat the following for arbitrarily many times determined by A: In each round, A has 2 options.
  - $\mathcal{A}$  can submit  $h \in H, y \in Y_P$  to delegate  $\mathsf{sk}_h$  with y. Upon this query, let  $h' \leftarrow |H|$  and set  $H \leftarrow H \cup \{h'\}$ . Define  $Z_{h'} = (Z_h, y)$  and run  $\mathsf{sk}_{h'} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^\lambda, \mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{sk}_h, y)$ . Resume the experiment without returning any information to  $\mathcal{A}$ .
  - $\mathcal{A}$  can submit  $h \in \mathcal{H}$  to have  $\mathsf{sk}_h$  revealed. Upon this query, let  $R \leftarrow R \cup \{h\}$  and return  $\mathsf{sk}_h$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .
- Challenge.  $\mathcal{A}$  submits  $x \in X_P, g_0, g_1 \in M_{\lambda}$ . Run ct  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Enc}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{mpk}, x, g_b)$  and return ct to  $\mathcal{A}$ .
- Query II. Same as Query I.
- Guess.  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs a bit b'. The outcome of the experiment is b' if for all  $h \in \mathbb{R}$ , there exists y in  $\mathbb{Z}_h$  such that P(x,y) = 0. Otherwise, the outcome is 0.

Our approach to ABE supporting delegation builds on top of the basic ABE and adds two ingredients: i) secret-sharing the pads to support conjunction of policies; ii) key-homomorphism from IPFE to support generating delegated keys without master secret key. We first discuss them separately, then put them together to obtain the ABE scheme supporting delegation.

### <span id="page-90-0"></span>**B.1 1-ABE Supporting Conjunctions**

To construct ABE supporting delegation, secret keys must be capable of expressing arbitrary conjunction of policies in  $Y_P$ . We start by showing how to tweak 1-ABE (Construction 24) to achieve this. To begin, we make these modifications to 1-ABE and its security definition:

- A 1-ABE supporting conjunction for predicate space  $\mathcal{P}$  has the same syntax as a usual 1-ABE, except that KeyGen now takes in an arbitrary number of policies  $y_1, \ldots, y_D \in Y_P$ , and generates a key for them, and that Dec must decapsulate the pad only when  $P(x, y_1) = \cdots = P(x, y_D) = 1$ .
- In 1-key 1-ciphertext security experiment, the adversary submits an arbitrary number of policies (in one shot) to request a secret key for the conjunction of them. The restriction on the challenge attribute is that there exists  $y_d \in \{y_1, \ldots, y_D\}$  (among the submitted policies) such that  $P(x, y_d) = 0$ .

Tweaking Construction 24. To support conjunctions, KeyGen secret-shares the pad  $\mu$  to encapsulate into  $\mu_1, \ldots, \mu_D$  among the policies, and garbles  $y_d$  with pad  $\mu_d$ . To decapsulate the pad, Dec first recovers each share  $\mu_d$  in the exponent, then sums them to get  $\mu$ .

Formally, recall that in Construction 24, the predicate space is induced by some function class  $\mathcal{F}$  with AKGS (Garble, Eval):

$$X_n = \mathbb{Z}_p^n, \quad Y_n = \{ f_{\neq 0}, f_{=0} \mid f \in \mathcal{F}, f : \mathbb{Z}_p^n \to \mathbb{Z}_p \},$$
  
$$\mathcal{P} = \{ P_n : X_n \times Y_n \to \{0, 1\}, (\mathbf{x}, y) \mapsto y(\mathbf{x}) \mid n \in \mathbb{N} \}.$$

 $\mathsf{Setup}(1^n)$  simply runs  $\mathsf{msk} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{IPFE}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^n)$  to set up a function-hiding secret-key  $\mathsf{IPFE}$  scheme as before.

The tweaked version of KeyGen(msk,  $y_1, \ldots, y_D, \mu$ ) first samples  $\mu_1, \ldots, \mu_{D-1}, \eta_1, \ldots, \eta_D \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ and sets  $\mu_D \leftarrow \mu - \sum_{d=1}^{D-1} \mu_d$ . Next, it garbles the functions underlying each policy, i.e., for  $d \in [D]$ :

$$\begin{cases} \alpha_d \leftarrow \mu_d, & \beta_d \leftarrow 0, & \text{if } y_d = f_{d, \neq 0}; \\ \alpha_d \leftarrow \eta_d, & \beta_d \leftarrow \mu_d, & \text{if } y_d = f_{d, = 0}; \end{cases} \qquad (\mathbf{L}_{d, 1}, \dots, \mathbf{L}_{d, m_d}) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Garble}(f_d, \alpha_d, \beta_d).$$

Then, the algorithm generates IPFE secret keys  $isk_{d,j} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} IPFE.KeyGen(msk, \mathbf{v}_{d,j})$  to encrypt the coefficient vectors:

It returns  $\mathsf{sk} = (y_1, \mathsf{isk}_{1,1}, \dots, \mathsf{isk}_{1,m_1}; \dots; y_D, \mathsf{isk}_{D,1}, \dots, \mathsf{isk}_{D,m_D})$  as the secret key encapsulating  $\mu$ .

Enc remains the same, and an ABE ciphertext consists of the attribute  $\mathbf{x}$  itself and an IPFE ciphertext encrypting  $(1, \mathbf{x}, 0, 0)$ . The decapsulation algorithm  $\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{ct})$  first parses

$$\mathsf{sk} \text{ as } (y_1,\mathsf{isk}_{1,1},\ldots,\mathsf{isk}_{1,m_1};\ \ldots;\ y_D,\mathsf{isk}_{D,1},\ldots,\mathsf{isk}_{D,m_D}),$$
 and 
$$\mathsf{ct} \text{ as } (\mathbf{x},\mathsf{ict}).$$

It returns  $\perp$  if  $y_d(\mathbf{x}) = 0$  for some  $d \in [D]$ . Otherwise, it recovers  $\mu_d$ 's in the exponent, i.e., for all  $d \in [D]$ :

$$\begin{split} \text{for } j \in [m_d] \colon & \ \, [\![\ell_{j,d}]\!]_{\mathrm{T}} \leftarrow \mathsf{IPFE.Dec}(\mathsf{isk}_{j,d},\mathsf{ict}), \\ & \ \, [\![\mu_d']\!]_{\mathrm{T}} \leftarrow \begin{cases} \frac{1}{f_d(\mathbf{x})} \mathsf{Eval}(f_d,\mathbf{x},[\![\ell_{d,1}]\!]_{\mathrm{T}},\dots,[\![\ell_{d,m_d}]\!]_{\mathrm{T}}), & \text{if } y_d = f_{d,\neq 0}; \\ & \ \, \mathsf{Eval}(f_d,\mathbf{x},[\![\ell_{d,1}]\!]_{\mathrm{T}},\dots,[\![\ell_{d,m_d}]\!]_{\mathrm{T}}), & \text{if } y_d = f_{d,=0}. \end{cases} \end{split}$$

The algorithm returns  $\sum_{d=1}^{D} \llbracket \mu'_d \rrbracket_{\mathrm{T}}$  as the decapsulated pad. For correctness, following the same argument for Construction 24, we know that  $\mu'_d = \mu_d$  for all  $d \in [D]$ , whence  $\sum_{d=1}^{D} \mu'_d = \mu$ .

**Security Proof.** We give the idea of the security proof and leave the formalism to the reader. Recall that in (the more interesting case of) the security proof for Construction 24 (Theorem 25), we first hardwire the reversely sampled first label into ict, then replace each subsequent label function encoded in  $isk_i$  by a randomly simulated label, until all the other labels are simulated and the first label is reversely sampled, at which point ict, isk<sub>i</sub>'s are independent of the encapsulated pad.

The security of the tweaked version requires that the encapsulated pad be hidden when  $y_d(\mathbf{x}) = 0$ for some  $d \in [D]$ , where  $y_1, \ldots, y_D, \mathbf{x}$  are chosen adaptively by the adversary. We first consider a selective-violation version of the security experiments, where the adversary commits to an index  $d^* \in \mathbb{N}$  such that  $y_{d^*}(\mathbf{x}) = 0$  for its later choices of  $y_1, \dots, y_D, \mathbf{x}$ . The proof of selective-violation security follows by the same argument for Theorem 25 — we simulate the labels for  $f_{d^*}$  to show that the  $(d^*)^{\text{th}}$  share  $\mu_{d^*}$  is hidden, and so is the encapsulated pad.

Now, we can prove 1-key 1-ciphertext security of the tweaked construction by a random guessing argument and reduction to selective-violation security. For any adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  against 1-key 1-ciphertext security, we construct  $\mathcal{B}$  against selective-violation security. Suppose  $\mathcal{A}$  makes the key query for at most D policies.  $\mathcal{B}$  first guesses  $d^* \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} [D]$ , uses it as the commitment, and then runs  $\mathcal{A}$ . The new adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  aborts if  $d^*$  is not the smallest d such that  $y_d(\mathbf{x}) = 0$  for  $y_1, \ldots, y_D, \mathbf{x}$  chosen adaptively by  $\mathcal{A}$ . This reduction loses a factor of D in the advantage.

## <span id="page-92-0"></span>**B.2** Perfectly Key-Homomorphic IPFE

In our KP-ABE supporting delegation, the secret key has structures similar to that in the usual KP-ABE (an IPFE key for computing the sum of the pads) with the additional features in the tweaked 1-ABE (one set of IPFE keys for each policy for computing the labels). To be able to delegate, one must be able to generate new IPFE secret keys using the (new) master public key. Furthermore, we will rerandomize the delegated key so that it is identically distributed to a freshly generated one, with randomness independent of the key being delegated — this ensures that a delegated key leaks no information about the randomness in the key from which it is created, thus making security proof simpler. We rely on an additional property, called key-homomorphism, of the slotted IPFE scheme to achieve the above.

**Definition 56** (perfect key-homomorphism). A slotted IPFE scheme (Setup, KeyGen, Enc, Dec, SlotEnc) based on pairing groups of order  $p = p(\lambda)$  is (perfectly) key-homomorphic if it is endowed with an efficient algorithm Subtract( $1^{\lambda}$ , mpk, sk<sub>1</sub>, sk<sub>2</sub>) that takes the master public key and two secret keys sk<sub>1</sub>, sk<sub>2</sub> as input, and outputs a new secret key sk<sub>3</sub> encrypting the difference of the vectors encrypted in sk<sub>1</sub>, sk<sub>2</sub>. Formally, for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all disjoint index sets  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}}$ ,  $\mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}}$  (let  $\mathfrak{s} = \mathfrak{s}_{\text{pub}} \cup \mathfrak{s}_{\text{priv}}$ ) and all  $\mathbf{v}_1, \mathbf{v}_2 \in \mathbb{Z}_{p(\lambda)}^{\mathfrak{s}}$ , the following distributions are identical:

$$\begin{cases} (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}) & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, \mathfrak{S}_{\mathsf{pub}}, \mathfrak{S}_{\mathsf{priv}}) \\ & \mathsf{sk}_1 & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, \llbracket \mathbf{v}_1 \rrbracket_2) \\ & \mathsf{sk}_2 & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, \llbracket \mathbf{v}_2 \rrbracket_2) \\ & \mathsf{sk}_3 & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{Subtract}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{sk}_1, \mathsf{sk}_2) \end{cases} : (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}, \mathsf{sk}_1, \mathsf{sk}_2, \mathsf{sk}_3) \\ \begin{cases} (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}) & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, \mathfrak{S}_{\mathsf{pub}}, \mathfrak{S}_{\mathsf{priv}}) \\ & \mathsf{sk}_1 & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, \llbracket \mathbf{v}_1 \rrbracket_2) \\ & \mathsf{sk}_2 & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, \llbracket \mathbf{v}_2 \rrbracket_2) \\ & \mathsf{sk}_3 & \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{msk}, \llbracket \mathbf{v}_1 - \mathbf{v}_2 \rrbracket_2) \end{cases} : (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}, \mathsf{sk}_1, \mathsf{sk}_2, \mathsf{sk}_3) \\ \end{cases} .$$

Note that a key-homomorphic and function-hiding IPFE must use a randomized subtraction algorithm. For a key-homomorphic IPFE, given secret keys  $\mathsf{sk}_1, \mathsf{sk}_2$  and coefficient  $k \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , we abuse the notation  $\mathsf{sk}_1 + k\mathsf{sk}_2$  for the implicit application of Subtract to compute a secret key for the linearly combined vector.

Achieving Key-Homomorphism. It turns out the IPFE scheme in Construction 52 can be made prefectly key-homomorphic by publishing some additional components in mpk. First, notice that the secret key in that scheme is linear in  $(\mathbf{v}_{\text{pub}}, \mathbf{v}_{\text{priv}}, \mathbf{s})$ , where  $\mathbf{v}_{\text{pub}}, \mathbf{v}_{\text{priv}}$  are the public/private part of the vector and  $\mathbf{s}$  is the randomness used by the (inner) scheme. Therefore, to subtract two

keys, it suffices to subtract the group elements and add a random key of **0**. The internal workings opened up, Setup of the IPFE scheme samples

$$\mathbf{A}_{\mathsf{in}}, \mathbf{A}_{\mathsf{out}} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times (k+1)}, \quad \mathbf{W}_{\mathsf{in}} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(k+1) \times 2n_{\mathrm{priv}}}, \quad \mathbf{W}_{\mathsf{out}} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(k+1) \times (n_{\mathrm{pub}} + (k+1) + 2n_{\mathrm{priv}})}.$$

Unwinding the abstraction, a secret key for 0 in the scheme has the following form:

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{sk} &= \mathsf{KeyGen}_{\mathsf{out}}([\![\mathbf{0}_{n_{\mathsf{pub}}}]\!]_2, \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{in}}([\![\mathbf{0}_{2n_{\mathsf{priv}}}]\!]_2)) = \mathsf{KeyGen}_{\mathsf{out}}([\![\mathbf{0}_{n_{\mathsf{pub}}}]\!]_2, [\![\mathbf{A}_{\mathsf{in}}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{s}]\!]_2, [\![\mathbf{W}_{\mathsf{in}}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{A}_{\mathsf{in}}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{s}]\!]_2) \\ &= \begin{bmatrix} -\mathbf{W}_{\mathsf{out}} \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{0}_{n_{\mathsf{pub}}} \\ \mathbf{A}_{\mathsf{in}}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{s} \\ \mathbf{W}_{\mathsf{in}}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{A}_{\mathsf{in}}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{s} \\ \mathbf{A}_{\mathsf{in}}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{s} \end{bmatrix} \\ &= \begin{bmatrix} -\mathbf{W}_{\mathsf{out}}\mathbf{Z} \\ \mathbf{Z} \end{bmatrix} \end{bmatrix}_2 \mathbf{s}, \end{split}$$

where 
$$\mathbf{Z} = \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{0}_{n_{\text{pub}} \times k} \\ \mathbf{A}_{\text{in}}^{\mathsf{T}} \\ \mathbf{W}_{\text{in}}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{A}_{\text{in}}^{\mathsf{T}} \end{pmatrix} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n_{\text{pub}} + (k+1) + 2n_{\text{priv}}) \times k} \text{ and } \mathbf{s} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k.$$
 Therefore, it suffices to set the master

public key as (the newly added part is highlighted in the box)

$$\mathsf{mpk} = \left( \underbrace{[\![\mathbf{A}_\mathsf{out}]\!]_1, [\![\mathbf{A}_\mathsf{out}\mathbf{W}_\mathsf{out}]\!]_1}_{\mathsf{mpk}_\mathsf{out}}, \!\! \left[\![\![-\mathbf{W}_\mathsf{out}\mathbf{Z}]\!]_2, [\![\mathbf{Z}]\!]_2\right] \right).$$

The master secret key stays the same as in Construction 52. Subtract( $mpk, sk_1, sk_2$ ) computes

$$\underbrace{\mathsf{sk}_1 - \mathsf{sk}_2}_{\text{group operation}} + \begin{pmatrix} \llbracket -\mathbf{W}_{\mathsf{out}} \mathbf{Z} \rrbracket_2 \\ \llbracket \mathbf{Z} \rrbracket_2 \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{s} \quad \text{with} \quad \mathbf{s} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$$

as the resultant key.

**Security Revisited.** We observe that the newly published information does not jeopardize the function-hiding property. Again we just give the ideas and leave the formalism to the reader.

Recall that in the security proof of Construction 52 (Theorem 53), between each pair of neighboring hybrids, we rely on either the IND-CPA security of the inner scheme or that of the outer scheme. It suffices to show that in either case, the reduction algorithm can efficiently obtain the newly added components,  $[-\mathbf{W}_{\text{out}}\mathbf{Z}]_2$  and  $[\mathbf{Z}]_2$ . First, note that the entries of  $\mathbf{Z}$  are either 0 or from  $\mathbf{A}_{\text{in}}, \mathbf{A}_{\text{in}}\mathbf{W}_{\text{in}}$ , the master public key of the inner scheme.

If the indistinguishability is reduced to the IND-CPA security of the inner scheme, the reduction algorithm receives  $\mathsf{mpk}_\mathsf{in}$  from the CPA experiments of the inner scheme, from which it can form  $[\![\mathbf{Z}]\!]_2$ . It sets up the outer scheme by itself, so it knows  $\mathbf{W}_\mathsf{out}$  and can compute  $-\mathbf{W}_\mathsf{out}[\![\mathbf{Z}]\!]_2$  efficiently.

If the indistinguishability is reduced to the IND-CPA security of the outer scheme, the reduction algorithm sets up the inner scheme itself and knows  $\mathbf{A}_{in}$ ,  $\mathbf{W}_{in}$  thus  $\mathbf{Z}$ . It also receives  $\mathsf{mpk}_{out}$  from the CPA experiments of the outer scheme. The reduction algorithm then requests secret keys for each column of  $[\![\mathbf{Z}]\!]_2$  from the outer scheme, which gives it exactly  $[\![-\mathbf{W}_{out}\mathbf{Z}]\!]_2$ . This will not violate the constraints of the CPA experiments, because any vector for which the reduction algorithm requests a ciphertext will have inner product 0 with the columns of  $\mathbf{Z}$ —recall that each column of  $\mathbf{Z}$  is a possible vector fed into  $\mathsf{KeyGen}_{out}$  when generating a secret key of  $\mathbf{0}$ , that the reduction algorithm only requests ciphertexts for vectors that are potentially fed into  $\mathsf{Enc}_{out}$  when generating ciphertexts in the slotted scheme, and that the inner product must be 0 by the correctness of the slotted scheme.

### <span id="page-94-0"></span>**B.3** KP-ABE Supporting Delegation

We now combine secret-sharing of the pads and key-homomorphism of IPFE to obtain ABE supporting delegation. Recall that in our basic ABE scheme (Construction 26), we use a slotted IPFE as the underlying primitive. An ABE ciphertext consists of the padded message, the attribute, and an IPFE ciphertext, i.e.,  $\mathsf{ct} = (\llbracket h \rrbracket_T + g, \mathbf{x}, \mathsf{ict})$ , where ict encrypts  $\mathbf{u}$ , consisting of h and random multiples of  $(1, \mathbf{x})$ . An ABE secret key consists of several IPFE secret keys,  $\mathsf{sk}_y = (\mathsf{isk}_{\mathsf{pad}}, y, \mathsf{isk}_1, \ldots, \mathsf{isk}_m)$ , where  $\mathsf{isk}_j$  encodes the label functions  $\mathbf{v}_j$  in the garblings of the function f underlying  $g = f_{\neq 0}$ ,  $g = f_{\neq 0}$ , and  $g = f_{\neq 0}$ , decrypting ict to the sum of the pads. We refer to  $g = f_{\neq 0}$ , as the pad key, and  $g = f_{\neq 0}$ , as the label keys. The vectors are summarized below:

| vector                                       | pad                                    | $const^t$                                         | $coef_i^t$                     | $ $ in $\mathfrak{s}_{\mathrm{priv}}$ |
|----------------------------------------------|----------------------------------------|---------------------------------------------------|--------------------------------|---------------------------------------|
| $\mathbf{v}_{\mathrm{pad}}$ $\mathbf{v}_{j}$ | $\begin{vmatrix} 1 \\ 0 \end{vmatrix}$ | $\boldsymbol{\mu}[t] \ \mathbf{L}_{j}^{t}[const]$ | $\mathbf{L}_{j}^{t}[coef_{i}]$ | 0                                     |
| u                                            | h                                      | $\mathbf{s}[t]$                                   | $\mathbf{s}[t]\mathbf{x}[i]$   | 0                                     |

Expressing conjunction is done by having multiple sets of *label keys*, each for a share of the pad in the pad key, as demonstrated in the tweaked version of 1-ABE. Delegating a key requires creating new *label keys* with non-zero (potentially arbitrary) values at  $const^t$ ,  $coef_i^t$  without using the master secret key. A natural idea is to publish a set of keys for a basis for these indices (called the **basis keys**) in the (new) master public key and use key-homomorphism to combine them as needed. These keys do not help computing the pad h by simply using them to decrypt ict.

Naïve Attempt. A naïve attempt is to publish the keys for the natural (standard) basis in these indices. However, doing so ruins the security proof (see Theorem 27). To see this, recall that the first step of the security proof is to move the computation of the labels from the public slot of ict to the private slot, and s from ict to the isk's. Let us consider adding the standard basis keys for const<sup>t</sup>. In the security proof, when we modify ict, we must also modify the basis keys to keep the inner products the same. Pragmatically, the vectors will transform as follows:

| vector                      | pad | $const^t$                        | $coef_i^t \mid$                | in $\mathfrak{s}_{\mathrm{priv}}$ | ,                 | pad | $const^t$                         | $coef_i^t$                     | const                                           | $coef_i$        | group |
|-----------------------------|-----|----------------------------------|--------------------------------|-----------------------------------|-------------------|-----|-----------------------------------|--------------------------------|-------------------------------------------------|-----------------|-------|
| $\mathbf{v}_{\mathrm{pad}}$ | 1   | $1[t=t'] \\ \boldsymbol{\mu}[t]$ | 0 0                            | 0                                 | $\longrightarrow$ | 1   | $1[t = t']$ $\boldsymbol{\mu}[t]$ | 0                              | $ \langle \boldsymbol{\mu}, \mathbf{s} \rangle$ | 0               | $G_2$ |
| $\mathbf{v}_{j}$            | 0   | $\mathbf{L}_{j}^{t}[const]$      | $\mathbf{L}_{j}^{t}[coef_{i}]$ |                                   |                   | 0   | $\mathbf{L}_{j}^{t}[const]$       | $\mathbf{L}_{j}^{t}[coef_{i}]$ |                                                 | • • •           |       |
| u                           | h   | $\mathbf{s}[t]$                  | $\mathbf{s}[t]\mathbf{x}[i]$   | 0                                 | •                 | h   | 0                                 | 0                              | 1                                               | $\mathbf{x}[i]$ | $G_1$ |

Here, the expression for the label keys are suppressed for brevity — they are the linear combinations of  $\mathbf{L}_{i}^{t}$ 's with coefficients  $\mathbf{s}$  (combined over  $t \in [k]$ ).

The next step in the security proof is to replace the pad  $\langle \mu, \mathbf{s} \rangle$  and the label functions in the private slot by independent randomly generated ones. For this, we rely on the MDDH<sub>k</sub> assumption in  $G_2$ :

$$[\![\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{s}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{A}]\!]_2 \approx [\![\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}]\!]_2$$
, where  $\mathbf{A} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times N}, \mathbf{s} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k, \mathbf{c} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^N$ .

Here, N counts the total amount of randomness used in the secret keys, and  $\mathbf{A}$  consists of the randomness used in the public slot garblings. However, this assumption can no longer be used if we want to publish the basis keys as above, because the right-hand side distribution does not contain  $[s]_2$ , which is necessary to generate the basis keys.

Random Basis. To mitigate the above issue, we notice that for the purpose of delegation, it suffices to publish a set of basis keys for any basis. The delegation procedure can use the freshly generated label functions to combine the basis keys, which corresponds to implicitly applying an invertible linear transformation over the label functions. The combined keys still follow the correct distribution (having the label functions from k independent garblings), thanks to the linearity of AKGS Garble.

If we publish the basis keys for a random basis  $\mathbf{B} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times k}$ , it is valid to invoke MDDH<sub>k</sub> in  $G_2$  to rerandomize the garblings in the private slot. In the first hybrid, the basis keys will have values from  $\mathbf{B}$  in the public slot, and values from  $\mathbf{s}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{B}$  in the private slot. Now, we can apply MDDH<sub>k</sub> to argue

$$[\![\mathbf{A},\mathbf{B},\mathbf{s}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{A},\mathbf{s}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{B}]\!]_{2} \approx [\![\mathbf{A},\mathbf{B},\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}},\mathbf{d}^{\mathsf{T}}]\!]_{2}, \text{ where } \mathbf{A} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k\times N}, \mathbf{B} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k\times k}, \mathbf{s} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{c} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{N}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow}
